There is much significance placed on the importance of an enduring marriage and a stable home life for raising children; the undesirability and adverse consequences of extramarital affairs; and an unquestioning acceptance of the suburban, consumerist lifestyle - Ms. Bening's `Nic' is a meat-eater, with no patience for composting or recycling, and (as a good Californian) she drives the requisite Volvo wagon.      In the same way that the `Cosby Show' portrayed professional blacks as potentially just as conventional as their white counterparts; so `The Kids are All Right' shows that a lesbian couple can be just as mainstream as straights.  In fact, without the lesbian angle here, there wouldn't be a movie. The film is well written, and well crafted; the acting never falls below competency; and the production values are consistently workmanlike (or perhaps more accurately, 'workwomanlike'). Nic and Jules, like any married couple, have begun to bicker over small things. Nic, a physician, is brittle and controlling. Jules, who is softer and more subservient, wishes that she had developed herself professionally; she is tired of being looked upon as little more than a housewife. However, the two women are united in their concern for Joni and Laser, whom they treat with firmness tempered by unconditional love.<br /><br />Paul is the catalyst for a crisis that could undo Nic and Jules. Not only does this motorcycle riding, ingratiating, and laid-back restaurateur capture the kids' hearts, but he comes between Nic and Jules. Bening, as the edgy and bossy Nic, tries to hide her vulnerability, and Ruffalo almost steals the film with his charisma and easygoing charm. Moore is confused as a woman who is having a midlife crisis and is going a bit crazy. The profanity and explicit sexuality are off-putting at times, and the generally strong script has Jules behaving in a way that is not entirely believable. Still, there is warmth, wit, humor, and depth in this film that effectively conveys a universal truth: The bonds in any nuclear family may be strong, but they are not unbreakable. B003UESJHO|3.0|As is the case with many inspirational sports movies, \\"The Fighter\\" uses physical competition as a metaphor for emotional competition. It's based on the true story of Lowell, Massachusetts boxer \\"Irish\\" Micky Ward, who went pro in 1985, went on hiatus after losing four consecutive fights in 1990, and then made a comeback that lasted from 1997 until his retirement in 2003. His final match was a trilogy of fights with Arturo Gatti, but all events in the film lead up to an earlier match with Shea Neary. This change might have stemmed from a desire to make the film even more symbolic, Neary being from Liverpool and Ward fighting against the odds on foreign soil. I dare not make that claim with any degree of certainty. Watch the film and decide for yourself. I'm sure it can be interpreted in a number of ways.    In the film, Micky (Mark Wahlberg) lives in the shadow of his older half-brother, Dicky Eklund (Christian Bale), himself a boxer who fought Sugar Ray Leonard in 1978 and came home a legend. Now aging and in the throes of a crack addiction, we find a camera crew from HBO following him around, the director shooting a documentary about his drug abuse. He has nothing but his memories of that long ago fight, which may be why he feels he must personally train Micky as he struggles to make a comeback. But it's not merely a matter of personally training him; he never lets Micky make his own decisions about how a fight is supposed to be fought. When it comes to being in the ring, Dicky knows best, and that's all there is to it. Micky cannot get a word in edgewise.    It's even worse with his chain smoking mother, Alice (Melissa Leo), probably one of the least likable people I've seen a movie all year. She has appointed herself as Micky's manager and never lets him have a say in anything. For reasons never adequately explained, she deeply distrusts anyone who can train Micky professionally; reputable managers are out to steal him blind, and anyone who suggests otherwise isa traitor to the family. Behind her all the way are her daughters, seven of the most unpleasant, unlovable women ever to pollute the silver screen. Watching them with their mother is a little like watching a cult figure brainwashing her disciples. There's no better proof of this then a scene in which Alice and her girls simultaneously storm over to retrieve Micky from his girlfriend's house.    Her name is Charlene Fleming (Amy Adams). She went to college on a scholarship but blew it by partying too hard and skipping too many classes. Now she's stuck Lowell tending a seedy bar. She and Micky immediately hit it off, but the same cannot be said about her and Micky's family. Alice and her daughters are convinced, through nothing other than rumor, that she's a wild MTV party girl. Charlene, tough and foul-mouthed, has no problem telling off Alice and Dicky, and it goes on like this until a very unlikely turnaround near the end of the film, when miraculously everyone is in Micky's corner. In her own way, Charlene is just as controlling as Micky's mother; both claim to have his best interests at heart, and both say whatever they can to prevent Micky from consorting with the other.    Have you noticed that I've said a lot about everyone around Micky but not so much about Micky? The film is essentially about a man who has been pushed around and lacks the ability to speak for himself. In this sense, the film is effective; we long for that moment when Micky will finally take a stand for his independence and make his family realize that his voice is just as important as theirs. Basically, he wants to get along with his family and his girlfriend. Why should he have to choose? When Dicky is arrested and sentenced to eight months in jail, Micky hires a new manager, who makes him promise to never again train with Dicky. At first, Micky agrees, for the arrest resulted in his right hand getting broken. But upon Dicky's release, Micky releases that being trained by his brother is important to him. Again, why must he choose? Can't we all just get along?    Despite receiving top billing, this really isn't a vehicle for Mark Wahlberg. The spotlight is on Christian Bale, whose intense, oddly rhythmic take on Dicky is convincing. We have by now become accustomed to Bale suffering for his art, and \\"The Fighter\\" is no exception; to play an authentic crack head, he had to his lose a noticeable amount of weight. We have also heard plenty about his practice of staying in character during movie shoots; true to form, he retained Dicky's powerful Boston accent all throughout filming. His performance is praiseworthy, and yet it's also in service of a contrived story that hits all the predictable notes. \\"The Fighter\\" certainly has its good points, but I have an unshakable feeling that its claim of being based on a true story is a bit of an understatement.

In summary, this is one of the top 100 albums of all time for a reason and that is the masterful writing and playing of the Glimmer Twins. B000OLGCHA|5.0|I recently purchased and having been enjoying thoroughly, the soundtracks to Harry Potter and The Order of the Phoenix and Harry Potter and The Half-Blood Prince.      As a professional musician and closet composer, I have to say I rate Nicholas Hooper's score a 5 out of 5.  His music is original and yet well versed in the language of Post-Romanticism, in which movies are the most active medium for this style of classical music to dwell.  That is not to say that his style is completely Post-Romantic, just that there are Post-Romantic elements utilized in his music and much of the movie scores we enjoy in movies today.    The score is evocative, the harmony changes smooth and unpedestrian and the themes beautifully capture each moment in the movie and the personalities of each character/s depicted.  Additionally I enjoyed how the music was presented on the cd - as a continuous tone poem with very little or no space between the selections.    Some of my favorite themes are:    The Room of Requirement - 5th Movie  The Ministry of Magic - 5th Movie   Dumbledore's Farewell - 6th Movie   When Ginny Kissed Harry - 6th Movie    The Room of Requirement's opening chords are an homage to those who enjoy Ligeti.  Yet, they are so original I would only say that they utilize Ligeti's structure.  The sonic effect is Mr. Hooper's own.    The Ministry of Magic theme captures beautifully the hustle, bustle and inspirational atmosphere one would feel upon entering those magical halls.  Yes, I am not a fan of minimalism, but I enjoy Mr. Hooper's use and then abandon of that structure to achieve the desired effect.    When Ginny Kissed Harry is so beautifully simple and yet made so poignant with the introduction of the Room of Requirement chord from the 5th soundtrack.  It captures that gentle moment perfectly and never lets us forget about the magic that is constantly around the two characters and flowing through them.    Regarding the passacaglia that Mr. Hooper composed for Dumbledore's Farewell - the music is hauntingly simple and yet so beautifully complex.  He brilliantly executes Purcell's ability to have a repeating basso continuo and add one painfully beautiful harmonic layer upon another so that you are weeping at the end and longing for more.  The only downfall of the piece is that he had to end that it after a few minutes. As a movie composer, one is married to the director's storyboard - that is in no means a criticism of David Yates, 5 and 6 (and now 7.1 and 7.2's director), merely an observation of the structure of movie composition in general.    Segueing more into the structure of movie music composition and process - I was also immediately impressed how the director, David Yates, included Mr. Hooper in the very early stages of the movie's story boarding and filming process and was even able to play musical themes for the actors while they were rehearsing scenes.  As a performer of opera and an actress that also sings, this is the ultimate experience - to have the music influence your interpretation of your character!  I had never before heard of such a process and perhaps this is why I found the music and the story telling so seamlessly entwined with one another, unlike the other Harry Potter movies.  I will add, that I felt this way before I knew the fact regarding Mr. Hooper's composing process with Mr. Yates.    In summary, Mr. Hooper utilizes the best in classical music styles with his own original voice to put forward a beautifully executed and seamless score to the 5th and 6th films.  I was disappointed that he could not do the two films devoted to the 7th book. I hope that I will get to hear Mr. Hooper's compositions in the future and also in mediums outside of movie making where he would be allowed a bit more flexibility as in symphonic and operatic works.    Cheers and Best Regards - Tami Swartz

B004132I20|4.0|Reinventing a popular culture character can be tricky business--what do you keep and what do you jetison to make it work in a different setting? Sherlock Holmes wasn't just a clever lad; he had many of the hallmarks of a sociopath--a brilliant sociopath working on the RIGHT side of the law. Although Arthur Conan Doyle didn't invent the detective genre (Edgar Allen Poe created the first detective to use deductive reasoning--or more accurately inductive reasoning ) but he perfected it assisting in the birth of an entire genre of fiction that has impacted every form of modern entertainment.<br /><br />In Stephen Moffat's (\\"Doctor Who\\") reinvention of Arthur Conan Doyle's brilliant, iconoclastic consulting detective Holmes (Benedict Cumberbatch) is brought firmly into the 21st century. Like Universal Studios brought Holmes into the modern era before in the 1940's with the films featuring Basil Rathbone and Nigel Bruce as Holmes and Watson solving crimes during World War II in England. It was a stroke of genius on the part of the studio at the time as it made Holmes relevant once again.<br /><br />Moffat and his collaborators have once again taken the bold step of bringing Holmes into the modern age but also creating a Holmes that is every bit as contemporary as the time he lives in. Holmes meets Afganistan vet Dr. John Watson (Martin Freeman) through a mutual friend and the duo immediately hit it off. In \\"A Case in Pink\\" (an adaption of \\"A Study in Scarlet\\") Holmes is brought in by DI Lestrade (Rupert Graves) to solve a series of perplexing suicides which turn out to be the work of an unusual serial killer. Holmes must find out how and why this serial killer picks his victims before he kills again.<br /><br />Featuring marvelous performances from Benedict Cumberbatch and Martin Freeman as Holmes and Watson, uses the opportunity to reinvent characters that could easily have become nothing more than pale imitations of previous performers from Rathbone to Jeremy Brett who was often considered the actor that most closely embodied Doyle's vision of Holmes. Moffat effortlessly integrates the story of the first meeting between Holmes and Watson into a complex, fascinating murder mystery that puts to shame most procedural shows. In fact as much as I enjoyed the recent theatrical film \\"Sherlock Holmes\\" with Robert Downey, Jr., THIS Sherlock is the superior of the two with much more complex and fascinating characters placed within a more complex interesting story that effectively takes elements from Doyle's own stories.<br /><br />Visually dazzling featuring top notch direction \\"Sherlock\\" takes an unusual approach to Holmes and how his mind works as well--we SEE the words that occur to Holmes as he is examining evidence. While this might shound awkward it works brilliantly allowing the scene to proceed without unnecessary exposition dialog that might slow down the action. It's a gimmick that always works because it reflects Holmes attempting to solve the case giving the technique context.<br /><br /> \\"Sherlock\\" looks quite nice with plenty of detail, a sharp, nice looking high def transfer. The only flaw that I can see are problems with aliasing in all three episodes. I also noticed that facial detail isn't quite as strong as a contemporary high def transfer but that could be due to the use of DNR to allow for a lower bit rate. \\"Sherlock\\" may not look perfect but the series still looks quite good.<br /><br />Audio puts dialog front and center but the 5.1 mix leaves a bit to be desired; while it is nicely done the mix isn't quite as immersively as I would expect for a recent program like \\"Sherlock\\". I could be wrong on that count of course--it simply could be choice on the part of the producers to have the sound like this. Either way, \\"Sherlock\\" looks and sounds quite good even if it isn't a demo quality presentation.<br /><br /> We get a commentary track on \\"A Case in Pink\\" and on the third episode of the series. Having recently enjoyed [[ASIN:B000ATQYT2 The Muppet Christmas Carol]], I hoped my sons could enjoy (and follow) a gloomier interpretation of Dickens' classic that followed the original story closer than I had expected.<br /><br />Zemeckis employs the performance-capture technique previously used in [[ASIN:B000Q7ZO8U The Polar Express]] and [[ASIN:B001AII4RM Beowulf]]. It is hard to understand why though because only Oldman, who portrays both Jacob Marley and Bob Cratchit, delivers two unique and satisfying performances. Marley is gruesome (much more horrifying than the Ghost of Christmas Future), while Cratchit is endearing and childlike, especially so during his commute home on Christmas Eve.<br /><br />In summary, this movie is a mess and falls well short of being a Christmas classic. Dickens classic proves beyond the creative talents of Carrey and Zemeckis, who are unsure how to present this tale. B0041KKYEM|1.0|\\"Black Swan\\" is a bombastic, pretentious, middlebrow soap opera that struggles hard to say something profound about art and women, but manages to peddle hateful lies about both art and women. Men who've read too much Freud and have no understanding of women and teen Goth girls who wear black fingernail polish will embrace \\"Black Swan\\" passionately.<br /><br />\\"Black Swan\\" has no rise in tension, no complication of plot. The entire film is a bleak, dreary, depiction of a schizophrenic who hallucinates and wears nice ballet costumes. Nina (Natalie Portman) is never depicted as having any normal life or healthy relationships the audience might care about her losing. From start to finish, the film features tight close-ups on Portman's anguished face as she careens through one hallucination, and one dance rehearsal, after another. Since Nina's world is so relentlessly bleak, horrific - and unreal - from the start, we can't invest in her. We can't care about her losing her hold on sanity, because she is shown from the get-go to have no hold on sanity. We can't care about her losing her friendships, or her hope, because she has no friendships and she has no hope. Nothing changes. Nothing is at stake. Nina is as big of a messy basket case an hour into the film as she was at the beginning. The endless close-ups on Nina's anguished face get old really fast.<br /><br />The filmgoer comes to understand disastrously quickly that Aronofsky's bag of tricks is limited. The sets are all in stiflingly monotonous black and white, no doubt to show how deep of a film this is. Since viewers don't know which scenes are real and which are Nina's hallucinations, it's impossible to invest in them.<br /><br />The one bright spot in the film is Vincent Cassel, who relishes a bath in smarm as a ballet troupe's sadistic, manipulative, and very naughty director. He's the only one who has any fun, and God bless him for it. He could probably dance on the deck of the Titanic. With his French bonhomie and predatory amorality, Cassel is a cross between Maurice Chevalier and George Sanders.<br /><br />\\"Black Swan\\" tells us that women are fragile and neurotic and if they do anything remarkable it makes them crazy. Craziness, in women, oddly parallels male sex fantasies. When women go crazy they have catfights with other women, including their moms, they torture their bodies and others in bloody, gory ways, they begin to pleasure themselves in visually artistic ways, and then they engage in same-gender erotic expression. Most of the women involved are young, fit, and minimally clad. How convenient for any kinky men watching this movie!<br /><br />Is this film misogynist? Ask yourself this, would Aronofksy make a parallel film about a football linebacker who goes nuts because being a football linebacker is a big stress? Yeah, I didn't think so, either. Of course this film is misogynist. \\"Black Swan\\" is a throwback to soft-core women's prison flics and misogynist film noir like 1947's \\"Possessed,\\" that depicted Joan Crawford driven mad by passion.<br /><br />\\"Black Swan\\" struggles really hard to say big things about art: that the drive for perfection can drive you mad, that artists sacrifice for their art, that art and madness are virtually identical. This viewer just cannot believe that someone suffering from full blown schizophrenia could become the prima ballerina of a high powered NYC ballet troupe. Ballet is not the whim of an afternoon. Ballet demands a lifetime of showing up on time, getting along with colleagues, and successful performance. Someone as ill as Nina could not have carried this off.<br /><br />\\"Black Swan\\" wants us to believe the old, crude, invidious lie that art equals madness. It doesn't. There have been some high-profile artists who were also mentally ill, but that the public tends to focus on these people doesn't make art and madness the same thing. For most artists, art means years of unpaid hard work, dedication, self-discipline, and self-restraint. The creation of powerful art demands that the artist be, not crazier than the bulk of the population, but saner. Not buying into popular lies arouses envy and suspicion. Some people who hate art and artists are often simply envious of art and artists. And some people really don't understand art or artists and so they feel threatened and enraged by art and artists. It is these people who have given us the lie that art and madness are identical. Aronofsky doesn't do art or artists any favors by peddling this lie.hat the artist be, not crazier than the bulk of the population, but saner. Not buying into popular lies arouses envy and suspicion. B002SJIO5E|4.0|So what would happen if aliens came to our planet? And what happens when they are potentially dangerous, but happen to need us? District 9 happens. Of course we would create a quarantine, try to control them, and wind up exploiting them as much as possible. Especially when there are weapons that they can use against us. We would tromp around in the disguise of good will only to keep ourselves safe. It's not a surprise. It's self-preservation and one of the reasons us humans have gotten as far as we have. Still, it's important to critique these ideas and District 9 does just that.<br /><br />It is a story of alien life coming to this world. Their broken mother ship hovers over the city of Johannesburg. The government there eventually gave the go ahead to break into the ship and what they found were the starving masses of aliens living in refuse. They needed help. Unfortunately, they are not like us. They are also potentially dangerous. This mixture forces the government's hand to make District 9 where the aliens, nicknamed \\"Prawns\\" because they look like and \\"act\\" like the bottom feeding aquatic namesake, live relatively separated from the human world. The only contact they have are with dangerous gangs or with the committee created by the government to control District 9.<br /><br />The story gets complicated as one of those committee members, Wikus Van De Merwe, gets \\"infected\\" with a substance that systematically begins to transform him from human to alien. The committee wants nothing more than to harness him, examine him, and in the process kill him. He begins, therefore, a journey taking him straight into District 9 where he finds himself entrenched in the life of one particular alien named \\"Christopher\\" and his little boy.<br /><br />Wikus is initially a rather hard character to like, but through the transformation of his body we also transform our view of him. This does not come easy or quickly. He shows his human colors by working with self preservation throughout the movie, making us doubt what we should feel about him (as we should). One thing is for sure, our view of the aliens is steadfast.<br /><br />With the context of the movie heavily steeped in how we, as humans, deal with differences in our society this film becomes a critique of all living life, not just aliens. How we treat, view, and act to those who are different than us can never be fully understood until we take their places. When we do, it is only then, that we can grasp the gravity of the situation. Even so, some of us recognize these issues, but they are too big for individuals to handle alone. The movie continually examines this societal norm while giving us an action packed, interesting, and very different film from the regular box office hits.<br /><br />My main upsets with the film is its seemingly desperate cling to gore. They put so much of it in! It feels as if they were worried the story itself would not carry the movie well enough so they added the grotesque to make it more interesting and likable. Though I am not one to shy away from the occasional cringing moment I got bored with the extensive use throughout the movie. Because of this heavy handed use of violence Tania seemed oddly out of place with her continuous love for Wikus. It's touching, but completely out of place in the broad spectrum of the film. If they had developed that relationship further by giving them more screen time or scenes I believe it would have fit better. Otherwise, this film is a diamond in the rough. Rare and original. It remains open ended, as most movies these days do, but it is a good stand alone film as well if not for the critiques it provides.us doubt what we should feel about him (as we should). B0040QYROK|2.0|I'm not going to be leaping on the bandwagon full of folks singing the praises of this KINO \\"restoration\\" of METROPOLIS. Of course I'm thrilled to see so many missing scenes put back into the film but the quality of the \\"new\\" material is so poor that it is almost sad to watch. I fully understand that this 16mm print contained horrible damage and I am aware that no 35mm negative exists of this material, nevertheless, the so called restoration of this new material is sub par. I listened carefully to the explanations provided by the film's restorer and I'm not buying it for a second. The decision to crop the new material is not an elegant solution to a tricky problem. Blowing the footage up to match the size of the previous footage would have worked much better in most instances and been far less distracting. While I understand that no actual cleaning of the film can be done, there are much better digital alternatives available than what KINO used here. Of course, that sort of digital restoration would require a great deal more money and time and I suspect that in a few years we will see KINO issue just that sort of thing (with the few minutes of footage that are currently considered too damaged to repair miraculously included). This current release feels sort of like a rush job to me. I will say that the original score is spectacular and has never sounded better than it does here.     The extras included on this expensive disc are not up to par either. There is an hour long documentary that is more unfocused than much of the \\"restored\\" material in the film. Did I really want a lesson in German abstract art and archetecture in a METROPOLIS restoration documentary? Oddly, the documentary spends only a scant 5 minutes on the actual process of restoring the new footage and the rest of it is a poorly made rehash of the making of . . . whatever popped into the documentarians mind. I suspect so little time is spent on the restoration aspect because very little time was spenton the restoration itself. Just a year after the footage was found this new version was having a theatrical debut. Certainly a film in the deplorable state evidenced in this version needs considerably more time than that. The documentary also takes frequent slams at American distributor Paramount for their initial cutting of the film, overlooking the fact the Germany also cut the film just four months after its disastrous first theatrical run. If not for the excellent preservation of the Paramount negative, no first generation negative would exist for METROPOLIS at all. Of course, Germany might have been able to hang onto their own negative materials if theirs had not been bombed out of existance during that curious little attempt at world domination . . . nevertheless the documentary implies that it is all America's fault for the ruin of METROPOLIS.     The disc also includes an interview segment with Paula Felix-Didier who discovered the complete print in Argentina. It's an excellent interview. A trailer for the re-release is included and those are the only extras in this edition unless you count the booklet (which is again mostly a rehash of the previous edition booklet with very little focus on the actual restoration effort). The disc comes in a striking lenticular slip case.     Does the new footage make METROPOLIS a better film? Yes, some of it does. The HEL sequence between Rotwang and Joh Frederson adds a new motivation to both characters and the sequences of Georgy exploring his new life under the watchful gaze of Frederson's henchman offers a worthwhile subplot. The rest of the new material is mostly extended reaction shots and bits of running around. All in all, it dosen't change the film as much as you might expect.     While I'm so thrilled to see this new version of METROPOLIS I can't help but feel very cheated and I am entirely unconvinced that this was the best that it can look. If the German's would give up their stranglehold on the copyright of this film (they attempted to have the film TAKEN OUT OF THE PUBLIC DOMAIN IN THE US, were struck down in the court, and are currently appealing) and give the restoration of this film to someone else I think we could see a much better version. This is especially sad since they did such a remarkable job on NOSFERATU (another film where no negative exists, only damaged prints). They should give this to James Cameron and see what he could do with it. I suspect it would look much better than it does in this sad state.

It was also great fun learning about how certain songs and lyrics came about. Guitar players (like me) will love learning about his various tunings and tricks of recording. I always wondered how on earth he got that dry-as-sand tone on the guitars at the beginning of Street Fighting Man and now I know.      As Mick sang, \\"All my friends are junkies.\\" And they were. This former choir boy lived a famously dissolute life. Mr. Richards was a heroin addict for a long time and drugs played a very big role in his life -- and in his music. He frequently used drugs for fuel -- cocaine and heroin, mostly. Who knew that on an ounce bottle of pharmaceutical cocaine, Merck described their product on the label in such nosewatering terms as \\"big, puffy crystals\\"? He describes how heroin allowed him to work and focus for nine days straight without sleep. (\\"Unless you make bold moves, you never f****** get anywhere.\\") While not the lifestyle for everyone --and he states several times \\"don't try this at home!\\" -- that's just how he worked. Those who also use drugs for work -- and you know who you are -- now have the benefit of his expertise on the subject.  And for those who complain that he goes on too long about hard drugs, yes, kicking heroin is not nearly as much fun to read about as scoring hash from Achmed in the Kasbah, but this book is about his life, warts and all. Frank, extremely intelligent and ultimately a very sweet person, Keith chose not to apologize for what he did and who he is. Good on ya, Keef. Great book.

B004132I20|5.0|Despite the ominous signs of a one-year old unbroadcast pilot and the subsequent three-part series being broadcast in the dog days of Summer where TV channels usually bury their misfires while everyone is still on holiday, Mark Gatiss and Steve Moffat's smart modern day reworking of Sherlock Holmes turns out to be a major treat thanks to clever writing, stylish direction and, most important, great casting. That Sherlock works so well is in no small part due to an engagingly sociopathic Holmes in the shape of a superb Benedict Cumberbatch and a better than usual Martin Freeman's no-nonsense Watson, recently demobbed from service in Afghanistan with a partially psychosomatic war wound. While it's never quite as extraordinarily good as the first episode (the second being the weakest), it puts Guy Ritchie's recent blockbuster to shame in the smarts stakes.     Finding clever visual ways to show Holmes' deductive process that occasionally threaten to veer into CSI Baker Street territory, it's remarkably effective at transplanting Holmes to the 20th Century with credibility and ingenuity intact - for example, the watch trick from The Sign of Four is brilliantly adapted to a mobile phone that reveals far too much about its owner for comfort, while, instead of a 7% solution, Sherlock's reliant on nicotine patches when bored - and it's savvy enough about Holmes' stamping ground to show just how the one-way system in central London makes it possible for two people on foot to overtake a taxi in one striking action setpiece. In many ways more stylised than Guy Ritchie's take, with intricately edited montages, superimposed captions and occasional but sparing use of split screens, it never loses sight of the character - this is very much Conan Doyle's Sherlock Holmes as you suspect he might have been written had Sir Arthur lived and worked today.     Moffat's parallel tenure on the revamped Doctor Who does seep through in attempts to make Holmes a more dangerous figure who may just capable of becoming the kind of psychopath he hunts and in the through-line Moriarty backstory, as well as a running gag with various characters assuming Watson is Sherlock's boyfriend. Unfortunately Mark Gatiss' Mycroft - Sherlock's smarter brother - is too arch and League of Gentlemen a figure to quite fit in: he's more late Avengers than Arthur Conan Doyle, and Vinette Robinson's adversarial policewoman seems to be channelling the worst aspects of Billie Piper in her surly latter NuWho episodes. But the worst is saved for the unveiling of Moriarty in the otherwise excellent final episode, The Great Game - it's not quite the descent into tedious camp attention-seeking that Russell Tedious Davies brought to the Master in Dr Who, but the actor's absurd vocal gymnastics undermine some good writing with an attention-seeking disregard for reality that characterised the worst of the Jeremy Brett episodes. Which is a shame, because Cumberbatch is one of the very best screen Holmes ever, able to do so very much with (apparently) so little that it's like watching Einstein vs Graham Norton engaging in a battle of wits. It's something that dearly needs to be remedied for the much-anticipated second season, but it's still not enough to detract from the most enjoyable and believable new Holmes in years. Even at 90 minutes, three episodes is far too few, and a year for the next batch of three to be broadcast is already starting to feel like a very long wait.     Along with audio commentaries on the first and third episodes and a documentary on the making of the series, the main extra is the unbroadcast 55-minute pilot episode, and it's a good job it was never broadcast or it might have been dismissed as one of those nice ideas that didn't quite work. Despite sharing the same plot as the first episode, it's visibly underfunded compared to the finished version, but more than the lack of style or the rough technical edges it's never quite as sharp as it could be. Much of the backstory is missing - no Mycroft, no Moriarty lurking in the shadows, while Lestrade willingly calls Sherlock for help rather than being badgered into allowing him on the case - and the show has a somewhat more sedentary feeling with dialogue taking precedence over action and scenes often playing too long yet saying less. The scoring is much clumsier too. But it's also clear that there's gold in the pairing of Cumberbatch and Freeman and in the script even if it may need a bit more digging and polishing, and satisfyingly they've learned from their mistakes in the dry run: there's nothing that's been lost or changed that shouldn't have been, nothing left behind that should have been carried over. There's enough that's good in it to have made it worthwhile on its own terms, but thankfully for once good enough wasn't good enough and they came up with a future classic.     One curious side note: for some reason Hungarian gypsy music seems to have become the default mode for Holmes scores these days (though David Arnold recorded his themes long before Hans Zimmer started scoring the Guy Ritchie film). It works rather well here and doesn't have the Ennio Morricone/Maurice Jarre influences nor the odd Irish jig of it's bigger-budget rival, but it's a curious musical choice for one of the most British of detectives.

B001OQCV6K|3.0|One of those really bad ideas that surprisingly turned out rather well, Guy Ritchie's Sherlock Holmes doesn't entirely work but offers enough fun along the way for that not to matter that much. Feeling at times like one of producer Joe Silver's projects for his Dark Castle horror label before the budget escalated, it pits a two-fisted Holmes against a supernatural adversary who has risen from the grave (not the first project to have the idea: Holmes was due to take on Dracula in a rival project at Columbia, and it's perhaps not accidental that Mark Strong's Satanic villain is played as a cross between Dracula and Himmler). The emphasis is on action and comedy rather than sleuthing and intellect, but when a film includes a spectacular setpiece where a giant henchman destroys a shipyard and knocks down a ship (not a boat, a full-sized ship!) in an attempt to crush the world's greatest consulting detective it's hard to complain that you're not getting your money's worth. It's certainly no surprise that while the slew of Homes films over the past century have usually been reliable but modestly financially successful earners, this is the first to do real blockbuster business: it's a crowd-please and no mistake.     While it's good to see the trend of giving Watson his due as a man of action and intelligence continuing here, Holmes doesn't come over quite so well despite Robert Downey Jr's best efforts, the script generally limiting his deductive reasoning to evaluating the most effective way to beat an opponent (not entirely out of keeping with Doyle's stories) with too many of his other conclusions rather too elementary to convince us of his genius. Even a scene where he improvises one of his disguises from items he finds on the street doesn't work as well on screen as it probably did on paper. Instead the emphasis seems more on a Withnail and Watson approach to the mismatched flatmates that offers some amusement but never really takes hold as well as the BBC's engagingly ingenious modern-day updating in Sherlock. Nor does Rachel McAdams' Irene Adler really work here, never given a real chance to demonstrate the genius that made her the only adversary to beat Holmes in favor of a bit on Unresolved Sexual Tension bickering rivalry.     The real star of the film is the city of London itself in the age of Empire, which assumes centre stage as setpieces are set around shipyards, a half-built Tower Bridge, the Houses of Parliament and London's Docklands when they still were docks, and very vividly realised it is too on a scale previously undreamt of. The CGI may not always be 100% convincing and its flaws are magnified on the small screen, but if it had been attempted in an earlier age it would have required model shots and matte paintings that would have required a similar suspension of disbelief. Best of all is the was the film manages to weave the director's love of London lore into the fabric of the action, setting in the most vividly realised depiction of the disparity between the Empire's corridors of power and it's barely working class underbelly since Tony Richardson's The Charge of the Light Brigade.     A great Holmes film? Certainly not. But for the couple of hours it's on it's far more entertaining one than we probably had any right to expect.

0618918248|3.0|This was a very difficult book for me to read.  The hardback edition is only a mere 374 pages, but I quickly found that almost every other page was forcing me to challenge my beliefs by questioning them on both scientific and logical levels.   I consider myself open-minded and approached this book likewise. True, there are parts that I still strongly disagree with, but more surprising to me were the many parts where I was in enthusiastic agreement with Dawkins.    AGREEMENTS    I agree with his arguments that Catholicism is just cleverly disguised polytheism, as detailed on pages 34 and 35. Likewise, as I learned in a mythology class in college, the Catholic devotion to the Virgin Mary is just cleverly disguised mother-goddess worship.    I agree with his arguments against those ridiculous televangelists on page 32.    I agree with that there is a lot of waste in religion as shown on page 164. Certainly natural resources used on towering cathedrals could be better used elsewhere.    I agree that there are lots of Christians that certainly don't act like it, as shown in the letters on pages 211 to 214. To quote Gandhi, \\"I like your Christ, I do not like your Christians. Your Christians are so unlike your Christ.\\"    Because it happened to me, I strongly agree with his view that children should not become indoctrinated. They should be free to explore religion--or not--at their own discretion. This is covered in multiple parts of the book, but especially Chapter 9.    DISAGREEMENTS    As I've said earlier, there are several things I disagree with, but to me, the only one worth any comment is his view of God.    Simply put, the God that Dawkins is ranting against is not the same one most religious people believe in. This is because Dawkins insists that God must have had a creator.  He totally misses the point.   If God had to be created, then He's not much of a God, is He?  I certainly wouldn't waste my time worshiping a created God.    With this being the case, his strong anti-God arguments are like firing a machine gun at a tin can.   Whatever book Dawkins writes next, I hope he realizes that to argue against religion effectively, he has to use the definitions that the practitioners themselves use, not his own version.    FINAL OBSERVATIONS    Reading this book, I got the impression that Dawkins wasn't so much frustrated with religion itself, but the EXPLOITATION of religion--again, something that I myself can agree with.     I was a bit disappointed with his use of Gallup polls and statistics to further his points. Statistical evidence is about as authoritative as citing Wikipedia on your 10-page essay on Immanuel Kant in AP English.  Statistical evidence only holds weight if 1) You poll EVERYBODY that the topic pertains to, 2) Everybody answers truthfully and 3) one must realize that that poll is only a \\"snapshot\\" of that particular moment. So a poll from 1976 is probably not going to hold much weight in 2010.  As a further example, one could argue that the Amazon \\"star-rating\\" system is a \\"poll\\" of the \\"quality\\" of a product. But even this is misleading, as there are constantly annoying reviewers who write 1 and  5 star \\"reviews\\" based on emotion, not experience.  I don't know about you, but there have been times I've bought \\"1-star\\" products and absolutely loved them and there have been times that I've bought \\"5-star\\" products and wondered what all the hype was about.    OVERALL    In the end, I both agree and disagree with Dawkins. But more importantly, I appreciate his effort at challenging people of different religious backgrounds to question their beliefs and not be mindless lemmings.

B000I0QL82|4.0|Every year as a personal tradition I watch about 20 movies I associate with the Christmas season.  This is one of my favorites.  Made in 1942, it was one from my parents' generation and familiar to me from TV reruns when I was a child, so it is very nostalgic for a time period and a season.  Holiday Inn is sort of the older version of White Christmas; it even features the holiday song.  It was made for a generation saying a spirited goodbye to the \\"old world\\" as it prepared to face a war that would change life forever.  Many of those who enjoyed the film in the theaters when it came out would never live to see its remake a decade later.<br /><br />The black and white film is a classic of the genre. While White Christmas depends heavily on color, contrast and saturation in its filmation, Holiday Inn creates its effects of elegance and emotion through great attention to texture, design and camera angle, creating a film that is even more sensually lush than the brightly colored later film.<br /><br />The musical features a reworking of some old songs, familiar to those alive at the time, and the introduction of new ones that have since become holiday favorites.  The comedy team features song man Crosby as it did later in White Christmas, but here his sneaky side-kick is the brilliant dance man, Fred Astaire.  As in the later movie, Crosby is about to lose the girl but manages to pull the coal out of the fire at the end by being the \\"nice guy.\\"  The film is filled with songs that feature his very recognizable voice.  Almost everyone still recognizes his White Christmas when it is played during the holidays even today, and it is often featured as virtually iconic of the season in modern films.  Some of the dance routines by Astaire are considered classics now, like the \\"firecracker\\" tap dance and the very famous \\"drunk\\" dance.  While they probably seem tame by comparison to the more athletic and acrobatic choreography of the present, for the time they definitely pushed the creative envelope.  Anyone who appreciates \\"Dancing with the Stars\\" will definitely enjoy some of the dances that were at the root of the \\"classic\\" ballroom styles.<br /><br />The film again features the concept of an inn as a venue for the action, though it is now Crosby who is the owner, rather than the retired general as in White Christmas.  World War II, for the United States at least, had just begun, and there is a musical number regarding \\"Freedom\\" that emphasizes the military aspect of protecting it for the US and for others.  In this vein, the film White Christmas is a logical remake of Holiday Inn for a post-war audience.<br /><br />Christmas is not the main focus of the story, as it is in the other film, but the holidays in general and Christmas especially are brilliantly represented in set and song.  The patronizing racial attitudes of the period and certainly the \\"blackface\\" musical vignette will probably set a modern person's teeth on edge during the Lincoln's Birthday routine, but for the time it was probably considered very \\"tolerant.\\"  It is certainly a positive measuring stick of our social progress over the past 70 years. My favorite of the routines is the rather slap stick minuet for Washington's Birthday.velope. B0041KKYDI|4.0|Completely removed from Hollywood's adolescent fluff, Danny Boyle's latest feature \\"127 Hours\\" is an almost documentary retelling of the 2003 news story about a young hiker Aron Ralston who, his right arm pinned by a boulder in a rock fissure, had to resort to extreme measures in order to extricate himself. Ralston himself wrote a book about the ordeal called \\"Between A Rock and a Hard Place\\", which has won sympathy for his predicament, even though I understand he broke the cardinal rule of hiking alone: always -- I mean always -- let someone know where you're going to be. As I said, the style is pretty straight forward, though Boyle does indulge in montages to create certain effects -- the hallucinations brought on by dehydration, for instance. The movie's first carefree fifteen minutes establish the hero's absolute joie de vivre so that the audience understands when Ralston (portrayed by James Franco) is ready to do anything to protect his endangered life, miles from civilization, and completely incommunicado. (What a time for a hip young man to be without his cell phone!) Finally, faced with starvation, possessing what is basically no more than a box cutter and no other choice, he knows there is something he must do. One is reminded of the hunter finding a severed fox's paw in a trap, realizing the animal has chewed off its own paw in order to escape and live. Boyle's off-center, indeed edgy style is perfect for this type of story, and \\"127 Hours\\" is one of his best movies, right up there with \\"Trainspotting\\" and \\"Shallow Grave\\". (His futuristic essays tend to be somewhat  derivative, and I have to admit I was disappointed in the belief-bending \\"Slumdog Millionaire\\".) The solution to Ralston's problem is, yes, a little difficult to watch, especially when he reaches his ulna and radius and (not having a surgical saw) must resort to a crude procedure before he can continue. But Boyle handles the difficult sequence with a great amount of restraint and suggestion. Adaring director but not sensationalistic, Boyle proves once again in this story of survival and almost incredible courage that he can deal with unpleasant subjects in a polished and engrossing manner.

0307588343|5.0|I very much enjoyed reading A Colossal Failure of Common Sense by Lawrence McDonald.  His book provides a very readable, sometimes humorous, instructive account of the collapse of the market and organizational dynamics at Lehman which many will relate to from their own experiences.      In some respects it is five stories intertwined.  You learn how difficult it is, and how  hard-working and creative you have to be, to earn a spot with a major investment bank if you are not groomed for the street via the right schools and universities or through other connections.    You appreciate the fortitude of some of these traders to stand by their analysis either at the casino gaming tables or on the trading desk when the rapidly deteriorating position on a massive bet would make most men fold.  You realize how smart some of these people are and develop some appreciation for the hard work and thorough analysis undertaken to truly understand a position or market.  If you have never seen it before, you see how a handful of misguided characters can \\"pour on the coal\\" and turn a steadily performing company into a run-away freight train.  Finally one sees how difficult it can be to slow down the freight train once the few professionals, long sounding the alarm bell, manage to convince and activate a critical mass of their contemporaries to the looming disaster.

0307269752|5.0|Our book group chose to read Stieg Larsson's popular novel, \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo.\\"  Several other of the many Amazon reviewers of this title appear to have come to the book in the same way.  I had grown bored with the DVD based on the novel and thus had little expectation for the book.  Our book group usually does not choose thrillers.  But I was surprised because the novel is indeed a page-turner.  I couldn't put it down.<br /><br />There are at least three interrelated stories in this yarn of a book which is primarily set in Sweden early in the 21st Century. The two major characters are Michael Blomkvist, a liberal financial journalist who aims to expose corruption in large companies and Lisabeth Salander, the title character, a gifted but troubled young woman of 24 who is under the guardianship of the state but who is a whiz with computers and at investigation. As it progresses, much of the book involves the unlikely professional and personal relationships that develop between Blomkwist and Salander.<br /><br />As the book opens, Blomkvist has just been sentenced to a short prison term for libeling a major industrial tycoon named Wennerstrom. (Libel laws obviously are much more stringent in Sweden than in the United States where this scenario whould have been most unlikely.)  At the trial, for reasons that stay obscure for much of the book, Blomkvist declines to defend the accusations he had leveled against Wennestrom and loses his case and his reputation.  He and Salander investigate the case further as the book proceeds.<br /><br />The other mystery plot is much more interesting and well- developed.  When he loses his libel case, Blomkvist is summoned to a remote island in northern Sweden by an aging industrialist named Vanger. Vanger offers to pay Blomkvist a substantial sum to investigate the disappearance of his young niece Harriet in 1965, nearly 40 years earlier.  At that time, Harriet was a girl of 16.  She disappeared mysteriously after a festival, when the attention of the island residents was diverted by an automobile crash on a bridge leading to the mainland. Vanger has spent much of his life, since Harriet's disappearance investigating how she died and who killed her.  He has had no success. Blomkvist reluctantly becomes involved.  As the story progresses, Salander comes to assist him in this investigation and in the separate investigation of Wennerstrom.<br /><br />The story of Vanger is complex, chilling, and tawdry.  It basically involves the entire Vanger family, many of whose members live on the island and dislike each other intensely. The investigation of Harriet and her fate take up most of the book.<br /><br />The story is for the most part well-paced and carefully constructed.  There is a large group of characters, including Blomkvist's married lady friend, the various Vangers, the Vanger's lawyer, and especially Salander.<br />As the book develops, the scene shifts from one character and place to another.  Tension builds throughout, and the story can be readily followed with its twists and turns without getting confused.  There are some chillingly evil scenes in this book.<br /><br />The pace of the book flags towards the end. The Vanger-Harriet part of the story resolves substantially before the book concludes, and the focus of the story returns to Wennerstrom and his claimed financial manipulations and corruptions. This portion of the story is much less interesting than the companion story, and it drags on too long. It seemed to me anticlimactic.  The Wennerstrom story and the Vagner-Harriet story both form the occasion for a good deal of preaching on the subjects of sexual exploitation and abuse and large capitalistic corruption and irresponsibility. Ordinarily, I am wary of books with these kinds of ideological preoccupations.  But in this case the themes were integrated convincingly into the story the author had to tell.  Thus, these themes did not interfere with my enjoyment of the book.  I did not find the novel ideologically driven.<br /><br />This was a long, riveting book, which I took up half-heartedly but found I could not put down. I am not sure that the remainder of the trilogy is for me, but I found this book fascination and well-worth the attention both that I gave it and that the book has received.<br /><br />Robin Friedmanen. For most games, I can't see a need for it.  I've played many, many hours of [[ASIN:B00269QLH4 Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 2]] without ever feeling like I was missing something.  And I think that's because as far as functionality in a FPS, the Dualshock 3 controller does very well.  The difference is in a game that requires you to hold down the rear triggers for an extended period of time.  I've recently gotten into [[ASIN:B002ACFYUM Split / Second]] and [[ASIN:B002BRZ7JE ModNation Racers]].  If you want to be competitive in either game, you're not letting go of the pedal for the entire race.    This is a problem with the design of the DS3.  Because of the sloped nature of the rear triggers, your hands are going to slip and you're going to adjust by holding the controller tighter, which means your hands are going to ache sooner and you're going to enjoy your games less.  The PDP triggers lets you keep your foot on the pedal (or your finger on the trigger) with ease.    Here's a tip: you really only need the PDP triggers for the R2 button.  Order one of these to pop on to two controllers; or, if you're playing MNR, order two sets of these and pop one on each of four controllers.  You won't really need it for L2 anyways.    But I'm still going to knock off a star for charging so much for plastic.

B001BO184Q|4.0|I am writing this review in the running up to Christmas. That is why I would like to promote the eminence of this item as a boxed gift set--costing even less than a KFC Family Feast (some $30 here at Amazon, or below 20 at UK websites like MDT and Presto). An insane comparison? Actually not, as I believe most families would be far better off with Puccinian calories during the season holidays rather than yet more junk-food dittos.<br /><br />What EMI provides for this tiny amount is the complete Puccini operas on 17 discs, save Edgar and most of Le Villi. Indeed, Decca has a competing offer on [[ASIN:B000YCLR6K 15 discs]], priced even lower here (not so in the UK, though); its main strength lies in Decca's superior sound quality--astonishingly thrilling and lifelike for the 1950s--whereas Tebaldi's featuring in every single opera is not consistently an asset (e.g. Mimi and Cio-Cio-San).<br /><br />EMI has, like Decca, included only stereo recordings, which means that some of the most acclaimed opera recordings ever made--the [[ASIN:B002N4DZ30 1953 Callas/Sabata Tosca]] and the [[ASIN:B000063UM0 1956 Beecham La Boh&egrave;me]]--had to be excluded. There are certainly no bad recordings here, but at the same time few first choices: only the fabulous 1958 Gobbi/Angeles/Santini Gianni Schicchi and Nilsson's 1962 assumption of Turandot would qualify without hesitation. On the other hand, it would be hard to impossible to gather these 10.1 operas from one label and do any better in terms of overall quality than this.<br /><br />Manon Lescaut features Caball&eacute;/Domingo/Bartoletti from 1971 in a somewhat weird performance, mainly let down by the sound which is both one-dimensional and unnatural, making Domingo's des Grieux sounding coarse (cf his [[ASIN:B000N4SJK6 remake with Freni]] on DG)--although Bartoletti's conducting is at least one level below that of Levine and Sinopoli, amounting to the low point of the set.<br /><br />La Boh&egrave;me with Freni/Gedda/Sereni/Adani/Schippers was taped in 1962/3 when Freni's voice was purity defined. By today's standards it is a very good reading, but when compared to [[ASIN:B0000041TD Freni's 1972 platinum remake]] with Pavarotti and Karajan, it sounds a bit dull and under-characterised--which, on the other hand, holds true for any other version. All the same, Gedda's unromantic and essentially non-Italianate Rodolfo is a drawback.<br /><br />The 1964/5 Tosca with Callas/Bergonzi/Gobbi/Prtre is controversial in the sense that it offers a stereo remake of the infinitely praised 1953 'definitive' version, which caught the arguably greatest trio of Italianate voices in their prime. As much as I love that classic recording, I am exceedingly pleased to add the remake in far superior sound, Gobbi sounding possibly even more evil as Scarpia, Bergonzi replacing di Stefano as a sweeter Cavaradossi, and Callas outshining her younger self in delivering the most complete, three-dimensional assumption of the supreme dramatic-soprano role (naturally, her infamous high notes cannot compete with the decade-earlier performance, but they are stable enough not to ruin the show).<br /><br />Madama Butterfly cast with Angeles/Bj&ouml;rling/Santini from 1959 is a truly great recording (I do not worry about the close miking nearly as much as others do). The problem here, though, is that Butterfly may be the ultimate 'listen-only' opera, which is why there are several other great versions, of which at least [[ASIN:B0000041S8 Freni/Pavarotti/Karajan]] and [[ASIN:B000G03Q46 Scotto/Bergonzi/Barbirolli]] are overall preferable.<br /><br />The sadly underrated La fanciulla del West gets a very good reading from Nilsson/Gibin/Mongelli/Matacic, recorded in 1958. Regrettably, the sound is stereo but rather limited, whereas Gibin's Johnson does sound like a non-star performance. Even if Nilsson may be the greatest Minnie on record, I still prefer [[ASIN:B000174LUM Neblett/Domingo/Milnes/Mehta]] overall.<br /><br />This is my first encounter with La Rondine, here given by Gheorghiu/Alagna/Pappano. I do not have any quibbles with the performance, which earned the Gramophone Record of the Year accolade in 1997, but rather with the light, forgettable operetta music that does not show Puccini from his best side. That said, at 2 including excerpts from the Second Act of Le Villi as well as 'Morire?' for voice and piano (Alagna & Pappano), who am I to complain?<br /><br />Il trittico's three one-act operas are dominated by Pappano conducting the LSO and Philharmonia in excellent sound from the late-1990s: Il tabarro features an underrated Shicoff, whereas Geulfi and Guleghina sound mediocre at best (the latter's voice turns rather unpleasant above the stave); Suor Angelica stars Gallardo-Doms who sings adequately, if without much personality; alas, di Nissa's outright ugly La zia principessa ruins an otherwise atmospheric and well-conducted performance.<br /><br />Saving the best for second-to-last, indeed the last part of Il trittico: Gobbi in the hilarious Gianni Schicchi is pure operatic gold, with de los Angeles appropriately angelic in the ubiquitous 'O mio babbino caro', Santini finding the ideal balance between farce and seriosity, and EMI's 1958 sound surpassing the label's normal quality.<br /><br />Nilsson's 1965 Turandot could have been the greatest on record, had not Pavarotti, Caball&eacute; and Ghiaurov joined the [[ASIN:B0000041Q3 1972 Decca performance]] and Karajan not conducted the [[ASIN:B000001G9X 1981 DG account]]. Even though Nilsson remains unsurpassed as the ice princess, Corelli, Scotto and Giaiotti cannot quite match the Decca cast (only let down by Sutherland in the title role), and Molinari-Pradelli with the Rome orchestra are no match for the spell Karajan weaves with the VPO.<br /><br />If it is too late to get this set for Christmas, at least make sure you have it by New Year.not have any quibbles with the performance, which earned the Gramophone Record of the Year accolade in 1997, but rather with the light, forgettable operetta music that does not show Puccini from his best side. Nilsson's 1965 Turandot could have been the greatest on record, had not Pavarotti, Caball and Ghiaurov joined the [[ASIN:B0000041Q3 1972 Decca performance]] and Karajan not conducted the [[ASIN:B000001G9X 1981 DG account]]. B001UV4XHY|5.0|For the Harry Potter fandom the wait for a new movie has been quite rewarding. Deathly Hallows Part I, has very successfully built up the suspense and the curiosity of the myriad of Potter fans, who will now wait restlessly for the final climax of the series. Followers of Harry Potter and his two friends, who have grown up simultaneously with the fictional trio, would have found the confusions, frustrations and rage of the stormy teenage years very familiar. The plot deals with a lot of real emotions, the emotions and aches of growing up, of being challenged. There is jealousy, anger and empathy--these human emotions mark a departure of Deathly from the bravado and mindless adventures of the earlier Potter flicks.<br /><br />Harry, Ron and Hermione escape from the numerous attacks of the Death Eaters, let loose by Voldemort. The killing mission includes a high adrenalin chase of Harry and his seven look-alikes by the blood thirsty gang, which is a high point of the movie, visually. Potter and friends escape into the wilderness, but the Death Eaters' attacks never come to an end, and their goons capture and take them to the lair.<br /><br />The trio escapes from the dungeon with the help of elf Dobby who pays with his life in the effort to release them. Dobby's death in the lap of Harry Potter brings about the most poignant scene in the movie. Now Harry and friends come to learn about the Deathly Hallows, based on some symbols and their intuitions. Hermione's reading of the story about the seven deathly hallows is accompanied by a symbolic enactment, which is very captivating and will remain deeply etched in the viewer's mind for long.<br /><br />While the development of the plot is very intelligent and flawless, there are certain issues about the pace of the movie. Some parts drag, especially the lengthy discussions and deliberations. The many ifs and buts during the unfolding of the plot frustrate the viewer, used to the fast pace of the childhood adventure and games of the earlier movies. Most of the action takes place in the outside world, away from the all too familiar school premises. That's a welcome change.<br /><br />Finally the fights and misunderstandings between Harry and Ron depict the coming of age of the chums.<br /><br />The die-hard fans will surely revel in the movie despite the fact the director David Yates has taken a lot of liberty about his selection of which part of the book to include in the movie and which to leave out.  For the uninitiated, though, this random and non-linear selection may appear quite disjointed and confusing.<br /><br />Chinmay Hota<br />Author of 'Hits and Misses's. B002ONC9NC|3.0|\\"An Education\\" is just \\"a movie,\\" and by that I mean it could (and should) have been better. It takes place in 1961 London. There is Jenny, a very bright and lovely almost 17-year-old girl, an only child, in her final year at a private school, taking her A-levels, on track to Oxford. Jenny's parents, at first, are depicted as boring, stodgy, humorless, cold, grasping, and a bit dense, really. They aren't wealthy - Jenny's father works for a living - they are what we would today call lower upper middle class. Jenny's father relentlessly pushes her to study hard and excel, but his interest in education seems only to be a means for his daughter to meet the right sort of \\"chap\\" who will, through marriage, raise the entire family higher up on the social scale.<br /><br />At school Jenny seems to be the only student who shows any serious academic interest, as well as interest in art, music, and French, subjects which, as her father says, are \\"not on the syllabus.\\" Jenny has big dreams of a life well lived, of hobnobbing with intellectuals, of romance and adventure. In short, the opposite of her parents' life and that of the teachers at her school.<br /><br />Then Jenny meets an older man, maybe forty. She's a bit smitten with him from the beginning because he is handsome, charming, rich, funny, worldly, artsy, and there are \\"so many things he'd like to show her.\\" With other adult friends, they go to concerts, dinners at chic night clubs, art auctions. Jenny is just 16, but with her knowledge of art and music, her fluent French, her quick-witted intellectual insights and humor, and her genuine, non-jaded appreciation for \\"the good life,\\" she fits right in with the freewheeling adults. Her school performance begins to suffer. Jenny's parents aren't concerned, because David, the older man, has charmed the socks off of them, too. The parents think that David is Jenny's ticket (and theirs, too) to higher social status.<br /><br />Now it's time for movie spoilers - if you don't want them, stop here. Jenny begins to discover that David (and his partner, Danny) are nothing more than scammers and con artists. That's how they make all of their money. When Jenny realizes this, her first reaction is to leave, but David somehow convinces her that there are \\"really clever girls like her,\\" and then, not so clever ones like him who have to make their living as criminals. In an echo of what Jenny's father had said, David says that \\"money doesn't grow on trees.\\" You need lots of money to buy expensive clothes, go to concerts, and visit Paris. David says, with a charming self-effacing smile and a shrug of the shoulders, \\"This is who I am.\\" I would have expected this entreaty, as open and sincere as it was, to inspire Jenny to run, not walk, away from this man. Even if she were willing to overlook his taking advantage of other people, how could she ever expect to trust him? How could she enjoy the high life knowing it was purchased with tainted money?<br /><br />The reason is that Jenny believes the only alternative is death, a life worse than death, such as the life of her parents or her teachers. She believes that even if she were to go to Oxford, after graduation she would still face a deadly boring life, and all of her study and hard work would be for naught.<br /><br />Jenny quits school and accepts David's proposal of marriage. Jenny's parents are ecstatic. As mentioned earlier, the only reason Jenny's father wanted Jenny to go to Oxford was for her to meet and marry a fine gentleman. So much the better that she had managed to do so without the expense of Oxford.<br /><br />But then, in the midst of celebrations for the engagment, we discover something new about David, something which Jenny is not willing to overlook. No, it's not more criminal activity. It's the oldest of all stories, the almost trite bane of older men seducing younger women - he's married, with kids. In fact, David's happy little family lives right around the corner from Jenny's house. David tells Jenny that he was going to tell her when the time was right. He says that he doesn't know why she's making such a fuss about it. But Jenny has SOME principles after all. She realizes that she's thrown away a year of school and her chance to get into Oxford, for a married man who really had no intention of marrying her. You see, he's done this before, and some of the other mistresses have had children. As I said, an old story.<br /><br />What I would have liked to see is a new story. I would have liked to see Jenny choose to leave David, without the obvious obstructions of betrayal and marriage. She didn't leave him because she didn't want to be with him; she left him because she couldn't be with him. She didn't make a choice, the choice was made for her. I didn't expect this story to take the easy way out, and I felt disappointed when it did.<br /><br />There is a happy ending. Jenny repeats her year of studies and gets into Oxford. But one more note about that: There is no adult in the story who can provide a decent answer to Jenny's legitimate question about what all the hard work is for, and what makes life worth living, rather than boring. Of all the people who could have answered that question and helped Jenny avoid some of the mistakes she made, it was Miss Stubbs, her English Lit teacher, a teacher who really cared about Jenny and the most intelligent and level-headed adult in the film. But for reasons not at all clear, Miss Stubbs allows Jenny to go off the deep end.<br /><br />I give this movie 3 stars. I enjoyed watching it, but it turned out to be the \\"same old same old,\\" a young woman seduced and fooled by an older married man. Jenny should have left him for being a criminal. Unfortunately, the author spends a significant portion of the book doing this type of \\"rant\\" against religion (rather than God) to the point that it becomes tedious and boring to read.    - The misconception of evolution.  By definition, evolution is the change of inherited traits through successive generations.  This change is caused primarily by mutation.   For example, humans have the same 99% DNA content as a fish, and a mutation in DNA can cause a man to develop gills and breathe underwater.  If a cataclysmic disaster were to happen and flood the whole Earth, then the circus freaks with gills on their necks will live while the rest of us dies.   This is evolution through natural selection.   The point here is that the DNA that causes man to form gills already exists.   DNA itself doesn't evolve-- i.e. your descendants 50,000 years from now won't grow gills just because you like to *swim a lot*.        The author uses evolution too liberally.  Like most atheists, he takes evolution all the way back to the single cell organism, even when there is no proof that man's DNA evolved from a single cell organism  (this is a theory, not a scientific fact, as plausible as it may sound).   He also tries to apply human morality in the context of Darwinian evolution -- i.e. we are moral towards each other because of genetic kinship, repayment of favors, acquiring reputation, and \\"buying unfakeably authentic advertising.\\" -- and yes, much of this idea is not his own, and there's nothing inherently deep about the concept that your next door atheist neighbor couldn't think of on a Sunday afternoon.    - Rather than providing a SOLID, DEEP argument for the NON-existence of God, Dawkins spends much of his time \\"debunking\\" religion, especially Christianity.   The type of arguments he provides against Christianity is typical atheist:    1) I go to hell because I'm not Christian?  What kind of God is that?  2) The stuff in the bible sounds awful -- incest, bestiality, people killed by an angry God, etc... I wouldn't want my kids reading it.  3) I can't believe some people think the earth is 4,000 years old.    You probably get the picture: the author builds on the logical/rational fallacy of one's belief as a case against the existence of God.    If anything, the only good point this book makes is that there are A LOT more atheists (and hypocrites) than most people think.   There are many people, for example, who stay in their religion just because their parents belong to it, while they themselves don't really believe in God.    In this respect, the book makes a valid point.You are what you DO, not simply what you believe.   For example, over 75% of Americans identify themselves as Christians, and if this number were truly the case, 75% of brides would be virgins since they wouldn't do such a thing as \\"fornication.\\"    A quick look on the \\"Friends\\" sitcom clearly tells you that American culture is already an atheist one.    I do, however, want to clarify one thing about Christianity that this author is consistently clueless about.   A Christian is not someone who accepts Jesus as his God, but someone whom Jesus accepts as his follower.  This is the difference between faith that comes from humility, and one that comes from self-righteousness.   Self-righteousness causes wars, not religion nor God.  Truth doesn't lie in religion but in the person who accepts it.

0060786523|5.0|Although I appreciate and even treasure many experimental and ultra-modern novels, I must confess there is nothing like reading a long, epic, XIXth Century-style novel, with lots of characters and subplots. This extremely long one, in which action develops between 1951 and 1952 (compare to much longer spans, like in \\"War and Peace\\" or \\"The Magic Mountain\\"), recreates Indian society in the years immediately following Independence and partition between India and Pakistan, a traumatic birth for a huge nation. It recounts the stories of four intricately connected families, one of them from the Muslim aristocracy, and the other three from Hindu upper and upper-middle class. Through this narrative, the author elaborates on different religious, social, economic, and political matters, very much still present in contemporary India. It goes literally and in depth on issues such as the shoe industry, agrarian reform, or burial habits.     The novel has two main threads: the first is the desperate search, by Mrs. Rupa Mehra, widow, of a suitable boy for her daughter Lata. This search enables the author to explore different circles of society, from the said shoe industry to the lives of spoiled heirs or the deep divisions between Hindus and Muslims. The other thread is the creation and approval, in the fictional state of Purva Pradesh, of a Zamindari Law, an agrarian reform that would take away big chunks of land from enormous estates, in order to distribute them among the peasants that had tilled them for many years (there were laws like this at that time). This thread, in particular, portrays the main political debates of the age, focusing on the contentious relationship between two old friends, Mahesh Kapoor, the State's Minister of Revenue (author and proponent of the Zamindari Law), and the Nawab Sahib Khan, Muslim icon and the largest landlord. They are the heads of two of the main families of the novel. Their relationship is further complicated when Kapoor's son (one ofthe possible \\"suitable boys\\") gets involved with one courtisan, the lover of Sahib Khan. There is no point in commenting the particulars of this thread, other than to emphasize that it gives the novel a rich, colorful context which puts in perspective the personal dilemmas of the characters. It gives the story its epic sound.    All those characters, and many more, have to do at several moments with Mrs. Mehra's search for an adequate son in law. The first candidate is Maan Kapoor, an obvious one since his brother is already married to Lata's sister. Actually, the novel starts with that wedding. His downside is that he is a lazy, spoiled playboy. On her own initiative, Lata falls in love, or at least into deep infatuation, with Kabir, a handsome and smart... Muslim. And through Mrs. Mehra's own toils, there appears one Haresh Khanna, likable and exasperating, an egocentrist but efficient technician of the shoe industry, perseverantly looking for a job that matches his ego and allows him to make a good marriage.    There are many other characters, and precisely one of the strongest points of this novel is that none of them is disposable or wasted, for each of them adds a touch of colour and information on the background of this tumultuous era in a diverse country. No chapter is a filler, all of them make a point in a very engaging maner. In fact, Vikram Seth achieves a major feat by keeping the reader's attention not only intact, but growing, through more than 1,400 pages of sustained quality. It is, as I said, a XIXth Century novel, at the same time epic and intimate, serious and funny, panoramic and focused on detail.    Take your time to a long, deep travel around a society with many cultures, food, clothing, music, and colorful habits. Go to wedding parties, attend festivals, be there at moments of violent political unrest, and find out who's going to be the lucky guy who will marry the beautiful, smart, lively and independent Lata Mehra.

Let me send out a caution to readers whom are scientific minded folks: This book is written by a journalist, albeit a very informed journalist, therefore, you should not anticipate sound scientific propositions.  What you should expect is a narrative account of the development of an idea that has a great deal of scientific data to back it up.  Although the data does not present a coherent, unified picture in itself, the author attempts to put the pieces together, and he does so in a well-written fashion.<br /><br />I make the comment about an idea going from sci-fi to science, because the idea of a holographic universe has been migrating toward the forefront of fields of science, such as cosmology.  Such physicists as Susskind and Hawking discuss information theory, as it pertains to information contained space, which was set into motion by inquiries into thermodynamics of blackholes.  On top of this, the universe as information has become an interesting topic in areas such as mathematics (cybernetics, etc.) and philosophy (Langan, Sheldrake, Wheeler, etc, and the conscious universe).<br /><br />In my mind, there is no doubt that Talbot was knee deep into the study of the scientific papers he had to read in order to write this book.
