0385523386|5.0|minor spoilers    Five stars as this was one of those rare books I found hard to put down. That's quite an achievement on the author's part as I find Jane Austen novels tedious, and would have thought to have been terminally bored by a book that's mostly about relationships within a group of women.    It's easy to find accounts of torture and murder of inmates by the State in horrific United States prisons - just check out Amnesty International's website. I'm sure you can also find lurid tales of sex starved female prisoners in shady corners of the internet. Piper's account, however, is of neither. She endures a relatively brief stay in a place that's probably benign by US standards. She only experiences some of the true brutality of the United States prison system mercifully close to her release date, and although she does refer sparingly to prison sex, her only sexual experiences are when she is groped by some of the male guards.     Understandably nervous at first, she soon finds friends among her fellow inmates, who are not the psychopaths that she might have feared. The book's main theme is the triumph of decent humanity over adversity, as the prisoners create some sort of life for themselves with virtually no resources. A yoga class is formed, Piper logs more miles each day on a running track than most free people ever manage, and numerous petty rules are broken as the inmates devise ways to whip up more interesting meals. Elaborate decorations are fashioned from practically nothing for Christmas and Halloween. Most of the prisoners seem to be far worthier people than their jailers.    A secondary theme is the prison administration's total indifference to the welfare of its charges. Little if any intellectual stimulation is provided, and the guards range from OK but lazy to nasty but not vicious. This neglect was sometimes amusing. In one of a series of useless talks supposedly designed to prepare inmates due for release for life outside, the lecture on housing was delivered by a nice but hapless man who discussed aluminium cladding and the best kind of roof for a house. Well, I suppose that was his area of expertise.    Piper seems to be remarkably unembittered from an experience that would have left me seething with rage. The only time she is really upset is when the State forbids her visiting her dying grandmother, but she doesn't seem to hold much of a grudge even for that. She's fortunate not to have any children, but describes the trauma inflicted on young children of prisoners who are deprived of their mother's care.    It's true that things were far easier for Piper than for the other prisoners. She only had to serve a relatively short sentence, had books and magazines showered on her by many wonderfully supportive friends, and had a new apartment to share with her future husband to look forward to on release. This only goes to show, however, just how bad things were for just about everyone else, most of who would almost certainly have fallen foul of the insane drug laws of the United States rather than committing any real crime.    I know that I should be far more angry about Amnesty International's reports on US prisons, but those things are so far beyond everyday experience that they're hard to imagine. By its very ordinary nature, Piper's account is far easier to experience vicariously.    A top book by a talented writer

B002ZG99QS|5.0|With a bunch of original-Tron viewers from 1982, Tron:  Legacy had some big shoes to fill.  And boy, does it ever...    The original Tron was groundbreaking in terms of its incorporation of CG special effects - the first of its kind to do so in a mind-blowing way.  Granted, the original looks horrifically dated now, but as I explained to my 7-year old daughter:  \\"This movie came out when less than 8% of the public owned a computer.  Think about 12 of your friends and then say, 'I'm the only one with a computer.'  Effects like the one in this movie (which has been completely exceeded in PC, PS3, Wii and XBOX 360 games) simply didn't exist.  The original rendering was performed with 2 MB of RAM...you were considered rich if you had a computer with 256KB!\\"  And Tron was a fun adventure story about programmer Kevin Flynn and his search for evidence linking his name to the creation of blockbuster arcade games stolen by his rival, Ed Dillinger who took over Encom.  Kevin Flynn is inadvertently transported to the computer world by the Master Control Program, an evil AI program that has designs of its own for the real world.  Kevin subsequently meets the security program, Tron, designed by real-world programmer Alan Bradley.  Together, they manage to vanquish the MCP and Kevin obtains the evidence he needs to establish his rightful place within Encom.    Tron:  Legacy picks up the story 28 years later.  In the past (1989), we come across Kevin's son Sam Flynn , a boy that has a great relationship with his father who speaks reverently of creating a new computer Grid where he, his own program CLU (\\"Codified Likeness Utility\\" - loved that), and Tron (transported from the original Grid where the MCP had once resided) worked diligently to bring about a new world order.  Kevin is somewhat cryptic about the \\"miracle\\" that he has found there before leaving his son to go back to work.  Kevin Flynn subsequently disappears leaving Sam as an orphan living with his grandparents.      Fast forward to the present and we come across Sam as a reckless recluse who seems to take delight in pulling off an annual prank on Encom (which has become a greedy, directionless corporation).  Alan Bradley still works for the company, but his shared views of Kevin Flynn's dream of making data management/sharing more accessible and affordable to everyone is obviously quashed under the current corporate machine.    Alan Bradley then receives a mysterious page on a quite antiquated pager (something that the movie does well in poking fun at) from Flynn's old arcade building.  Alan relays the information to Sam who discovers his father's secret project and, in turn, is transported to the computer world as well.  What follows is a wonderful story filled with religious allegory and reunification intertwined with bittersweet circumstances where CLU has usurped his own creator in his own quest for perfection.  I won't spoil the movie for you beyond that.  I will say this, however; many critics have lit up Olivia Wilde's role by stating that her purpose is to \\"look pretty.\\"  I will give this much up:  Olivia Wilde brings to the role a childlike wonder of everything that she hears about the real world with a great deal of heartfelt sincerity and believability.  It's almost hard to imagine that she IS acting it, because she pulls it off quite well.  To speak any more of it would be to ruin a potential plot point, so I won't.  I just wanted to throw it out there, however, because critics were unduly harsh.    I was 9 when I watched the original, but as a parent now, the pang of watching Kevin's son dealing with the loss of his father hits me square in the chest.  Like I mentioned earlier, the movie has grown up with me and it's a powerful nod and acknowledgment for those of us who loved the original Tron as kids and who are watching the current movie now as adults.  The story's theme is darker and certainly delves into the \\"human condition\\" and everything about the agony of suffering your own mistakes, however well-intentioned your purposes were.    There have been too many critics who have lambasted this film and I simply think that they don't get it.  Roger Ebert, at least, seems to get it quite well - he loved the original Tron and gives Tron:  Legacy a solid 3 out of 4 stars.  Too many others don't, however, and seem to feel that anyone who loves this film is a \\"fanboy\\" of sorts.    Well, count me in as a \\"fanboy\\".  Tron:  Legacy has created a beautiful story and the visuals are stunning to say the least.  The soundtrack is an awesome landscape as well; kudos to Daft Punk for constructing a soundtrack that is quite an impressive blend of both electronica and the London Philharmonic.  Thanks, Disney, for making a 9-year old boy smile again and for making our children shake their heads in wonder about a past that was yet to experience a digital revolution in both life and film.

Neither vocalist has a very strong voice, especially Ms. Scott, so the harmonizing doesn't work all the time.  Solo, each voice is fine if not drowned out by over-active instrumentation like on track No.2 Our Kind of Love, No.6 Love This Pain, and track No.8 Stars Tonight.<br /><br />My favorite track on the CD is American Honey, which showcases Hillary Scotts understated but ultimately pretty voice.  The track is vintage C&W: quiet, understated, soulful and emotive with just the right of C&W instrumentation thrown in make it an enjoyable country track.<br /><br />I do not know what the group was trying for in the Hello World (track No.4), but the song made little lyrical sense to me.  I loved the piano play in the backdrop of the track, but overall the song did not work for me.<br /><br />\\"Need You Now\\"  is a mostly satisfying mixture of fast (mostly misses) and slow tracks but it's on the slower more soulful track that \\"Need You Now\\" sounds its best. B000RZOR2U|4.0|To the best of my knowledge, this Telefunken \\"Das Alte Werke\\" set was the first ever integral recording of the Bach cantatas. It is not the first complete recording, that was Rilling, but Rilling never set out with the intention to record them all, it sort of developed that way. As a result, the gap between the first and last Rilling recordings is more than 20 years, and the later recordings, although performed on modern instruments and with women's voices, were informed by the growing knowledge of period practice. The \\"Das Alte Werke\\" set was the first set to seek a unified approach, and in addition to try to recapture the sort of sound that would have been more like the sound Bach would have heard, using copies of the instruments that he would have known.<br /><br />I have not heard all of these cantatas, but I know many of them from the initial LP releases I have in my collection, in their nice brown boxes (2 LPs per box) with miniature scores and detailed booklets. I enjoyed them, but, in my opinion, they have been surpassed. Nicky and Gus were trailblazers in the business of \\"authentic\\" performance (insofar as actual authentic performance is possible), and they of course suffer from the fact that people who follow reap the benefit of lessons learned and take things one step further. The use of these instruments was in its infancy and the orchestral sound is often thin and scrawny, the intonation of the valveless natural horns and trumpets often insecure. And occasionally I was caused to wonder whether some of these recordings had been made in Nicky's garden shed. In the now nearly 40 years that has followed, new generations of players have made these instruments their own. The Gardiners, Suzukis, Herreweghes and Koopmans have advanced the art and their orchestras sound much better than the orchestras in these works.<br /><br />In the interests of \\"authenticity\\", the \\"Das Alte Werke\\" recordings eschewed women singers, opting to go with boy sopranos and counter-tenors. Bach would naturally have used all-male singers. However, boys' voices broke much later in Bach's time, so Nicky and Gus are actually trying to reproduce a sound that no longer exists, indeed that no longer CAN exist. Having said that, the quality of the singing is generally very good, and some of the boy soloists (from various boy's choirs, including the Vienna Boys' Choir and the King's College Cambridge choir) are really quite amazing (e.g., the opening solo of BWV28). The contributions of the grown-up soloists are also often outstanding (Max van Edmond and Paul Esswood come particularly to mind).<br /><br />Having said all that, and acknowledging that this is a monumental achievement of the recording industry, I know enough about this set not to want to buy it, because to me the Gardiner/Suzuki sets sound so much better. However, I know people who absolutely adore this set, and who like the feeling of \\"insecurity\\", of operating slightly on the edge, of the whole thing, because this is what Bach must have been used to, considering he often had only a week to write, rehearse and perform a new cantata. These folk are the natural customers for this set. For anyone else, before splashing out this much money, I'd advise getting a single set of the cantatas (I believe the two-LP sets were released in CD form) and listen to one, to see whether you like the approach and the sound - and of course comparing with the likes of Gardiner and Suzuki, not to mention older recordings such as Richter, Rilling and Werner.<br /><br />One of the enormous (and to me enormously irritating) conceits of this set when it first appeared was the statement, more or less in so many words, that this was the only way to play Bach, and that any other approach was wrong. Hopefully, this has been erased from the record by now. This is music for eternity and should be played to the full extent of its artistic and aesthetic possibilities, not strapped in a stylistic straitjacket, using women's voices and modern instruments, if need be. I still turn to the old Erato Werner recording of BWV140 and the glory of the three trumpeting L&auml;ubin brothers blowing up a storm in Rilling's BWV129. Bach performance is not set in stone, nor should it be.nts, if need be. I still turn to the old Erato Werner recording of BWV140 and the glory of the three trumpeting Lubin brothers blowing up a storm in Rilling's BWV129. Bach performance is not set in stone, nor should it be.

B0041KKYEM|5.0|Black Swan is an imaginative thriller that keeps one on the edge of their seat.  The success of the film is due to the remarkable performance of Natalie Portman as Nina Sayers.  Her character is always on the edge, first to win the role of the Swan Queen, then to translate her cool perfection into the more passionate role of the Black Swan.  Her character is unsettled and going through a nervous breakdown as she tries to be the perfect dancer and cope with her own feelings of failure and that, once she s a success, that her life will go downhill until she is discarded.     It is interesting to compare Black Swan to the ballet Swan Lake. The original libretto of Swan Lake ballet had Prince Siegfried and Odette (the Swan Queen) die together, thus releasing the handmaidens from the sorcerer's curse.  However, the ending was considered too sad so the ballet was changed to allow the prince and Odette live and break the spell.  The film goes back to the more tragic ending.  The threatening bird-like dancer who appears at the beginning of the film is the sorcerer responsible for the curse in the ballet.  So Nina is cursed herself into striving for perfection that is her own undoing    The film plays off Nina's fears that someone will take her place in the role of the Black Swan; her represses sexual nature and fears that she will be discarded like the former prima ballerina Beth Macintyre (Winona Ryder).  Adding to Nina's insecurity is Lily who appears to want to thwart Nina to claim the role of the Swan Queen/Black Swan while professing to admire her.  Nina's mother (played by Barbara Hershey) is pushing her daughter into the spotlight from her own failure to become a ballet star.     Natalie Portman's performance is remarkable for the range of emotions she displays. She is on screen for almost all of the film, and it is her performance that makes the film. The supporting cast is also excellent: Barbara Hershey is obsessive in subtitle ways about her daughter's success down to a ballerina music box that plays music from Swan Lake. Mila Kunis was convincing as her rival, down the wings tattooed on her back and Winona Ryder turns in a solid performance as the spurred Beth.       The film is beautifully photographed and the ballet scenes are nicely choreographed.  Potential viewers should be aware that the film is very intense at times as we are drawn into Nina's hallucinations.  Black Swan is also among the most creative films in recent memory and is certainly a film that cannot be forgotten.

0393338959|5.0|I am a materials scientist. That means no social scientist: the things I am working on are deterministic in nature. You can model them, isolate given phenomena and analyze their exact influence on your system. Still, deterministic systems are very complicated to model. Therefore I have always been skeptical about economics, that ultimately hope to model the aggregated behavior of humans -that are inextricable, each one of us is unique and behaves most often irrationally (undeterministically so to say). I took courses in economics in grad school, and wasn't convinced either: simple assumptions that are seldom justified, conclusions that stem from rather simple equations. All in all, except by diving very very deep into macroeconomical models, I doubt that it is possible for any of us to grasp correctly economics. However, nowadays everybody has his or her own opinion about how economics policy should look like. Isn't it inconsistent?    This reminds me of two things: the theory of relativity on the early 20th century and climate change today. Theories that are developed by experts, people who have spent their lives working on a given subject and know their stuff. Still, everybody had an idea about whether the theory of relativity was true or not, and today each layman pretends to be an expert in climatology able to destroy the work of thousands of scientists in a couple of minutes. That is nonsense but due to one thing: ideology. The same goes with economics where different schools based on ideology rather than hard facts.    The very good thing with that book is that Stiglitz remains extraordinarily balanced and rational. His view of economics seems to stem only from his lifetime work and experience, and not from a preconstructed ideology that would deform and twist his analysis. He acknowledges his errors and the strengths of his opponents, but arguments strongly and convinces the reader in favor of his thesis: markets are central but there should be some space forgovernment intervention through regulation. He never falls into one extreme or another. Maybe because he does not serve private interests, unlike many other representatives of corporate-funded think tanks or politicians one can hear daily on cable networks.  He is a social scientist that has not been blinded by ideologies but has adopted an unbiased approach to economics, leaving research and the study of the history of capitalism shape his views. In short, it seems to me that he is an honest and humble researcher that has not tried to show \\"scientfically\\" that his preconceived ideas were right, but has let his experience shape his mind. To me he is like every scientist should be. And this book is to me a plain evidence of his intellectual honesty.    Freefall is not an easy read compared to other from his biography, say, Roaring Ninetees, owing to the numerous footnotes the reader has constantly to refer to in order to fully grasp the story. The somehow weird strategy adopted to introduce economic and financial concepts to the newcomer also makes the reading trickya, as definitions and concepts are explained sometimes long after having been used. This is why I would not recommend this book as a first read to the crisis, even less as a first read to economics. Partly because of the technical vocabulary adopted, and partly because starting right away with an analysis of the 2007-present crisis without reading about the 90's would be wrong. However, this is a MUST read for everybody interested in understanding what has led us to collapse and how we can avoid -or at least mitigate- future crises.    All in all, this book does not strike hard only because of its sharp analysis of the reasons leading to the crisis and the global view it takes. It succeeds in presenting a balanced view of the role regulators must play in order for the economy and finance to play their initial role, way too often forgotten: help our societies going forward as a whole.

0091936152|4.0|A Colossal Failure of Common sense looks back at the events of the global financial crisis of 2007 and presents stories, information and analysis that are intended to shed light on those events. By describing his experiences within Lehman Brothers, that author, Lawrence McDonald, is able to present the reader with possible reasons for why Lehman Brothers collapsed along with so much of the greater financial market.    McDonald faces many challenges in presenting such a broad range of complex material. In most cases, he succinctly explains the various causes of the crash including the role of low interest rates in spurring risky investments, the bubble mentality that took hold in financial markets, the failure of Lehman management to predict or prevent the crisis within the firm, the proliferation of credit derivatives that facilitated the spread of risk, and the failure of ratings agencies to warn against this risk. Although he describes these factors clearly, he is hampered by certain choices made when writing the book. By choosing to target a broad audience with little financial knowledge and by avoiding in-depth and data-backed analysis of events, the author only partially succeeds in educating the reader. In addition, his biases are evident throughout, making it more difficult for the reader to trust that the author is providing an accurate account of the crisis. Still, the anecdotes and inside stories from within Lehman Brothers make for an interesting read. A Colossal Failure of Common Sense is an entertaining portrait of one man's experience inside the financial crisis and viewed from that perspective, it is a success.    I have a full academic review of the book at my website at: [...]

0812550706|3.0|Ender's Game is rated the greatest science fiction novel on several top-100 websites, and that is how I found it. Midway through the book, the reasons behind this ranking became clear: its heroes are all children and teenagers, and teenagers are more likely to have voted for it on the Internet.    This is not to belittle Ender's Game, which is well-written as far as action goes. The book, the first of a series, assumes bug-like aliens are out to destroy the Earth and its colonies. Their defence requires an exceptional general, and he or she must be reared from the earliest childhood. So the novel tells the story of Ender Wiggin's struggle through spaceman military school, especially the battle room where increasingly fraught training is administered to young but not so tender saplings. And yes - without wanting to betray too much - Ender does find time to fight the bugs at some point.    Now the author, in his amazingly pompous introduction, says that the book was criticised for portraying children, or rather the exceptionally gifted youngsters who are his protagonists, as too adult-sounding. I don't think that is the case: his version of childhood seems to me tough but truthful. Where Ender's Game got less likely, however, was in its portrayal of the adult world, where press and politicians get duped by Ender's teenage siblings to the point that these become Earth-leading political figures. Nor do they do this through technological wizardry: we are asked to accept that an eleven-year-old becomes the world's foremost political writer and her brother an unparalleled diplomat. If that is a rather tall order, fortunately most of the novel focuses on Ender, nevertheless, and the rather lame political plot is marginal to the story. Ender's Game is thus worth reading for its battle-room storyline, even if it does not deserve the title of best sci-fi novel ever written.

006145205X|4.0|The author discusses several different aspects of the condition of Humanity - population, economy, food supply, etc.  He lays these out in the framework of a theory that Humanity is exceptional in the animal kingdom.  We are exceptional in that we specialize and adapt to change in ways that animals do not.  As such, we will adapt to changes and create solutions to the problems that arise in the world.  Because of this, it is in our best interests to allow the process to continue without external controls and interference.  To a degree, the author soulds less like a scientist and more like a libertarian economist. He supports the proposition that humans can solve their own problems and that individuals will find those solutions - not governments.  The best solutions will thrive while the suboptimal will fall by the wayside.  The proposal apperas similar to the 'survival of the fittest' or a social Darwinism, but the proposal is positive and optimistic.  The relationship of Humanity and the world is like classical economics - a self-correcting system.    The author supports his arguments like a scientist, citing both anecdotal and statistical data to support his conclusion.  He spends a lot of time discussing the situation in Africa and the issue of climate change.  He tends to see human adaptiveness and ingenuity as the solution to human problems and he has a lot of highly justified faith in the ability of Humanity to solve these problems.    At times, the writing is a bit dense, but he is generally very readable.  He does not assume a high level of pre-requisite knowledge, and he explains his case examples and his principles clearly.  It is interesting, but a bit hard to read at times.

0435123432|3.0|This is a variant of the epistolary novel, told by letters. Here, the story consists of progress reports made by a man who undergoes and operation to increase intelligence.  The plot involves his adjustement to his intelligence, the reactions of others, and his eventual decline.<br /><br />Originally a science fiction story, winner of the Hugo Award, and basis of a popular film, the story moves along easily, and would be good reading for teens or a young adult. The characters tend to be standard midcentury types--the narcissistic egotistical scientist who plays god with nature, a wonky graduate student, a bohemian blonde artist, and a status oriented but otherwise loveless mother. The theme of intelligence (which Charlie has in great degree as the plot unfolds) and emotion (of which maturity he has little) becomes a central focus of the character development, aided by Freudian type dream sequences, consistent with the psychology at the time of the book's writing. The author does a creditable job of describing the administration of psychological tests (Rorschach, TAT) to Charlie, in both his delayed and his brilliant states.

There is a jump from major scale pattern on one string to full the major scale patterns across the strings.  Unfortunately, this jump is common to most methods.  The missing step is how to turn the major scale on one string into the 5 common major scale fragments (in order from lowest to highest string VII-I-II, III-IV-V, VI-VI-I, II-III-IV V-VI and back to VII-I-II) and then how to reassemble them (in the order given) to create the 5 scale patterns (same fragment used on strings 1 and 6). By learning fragments first, learning all scales becomes easier to understand and you will learn them faster.<br />I won't be able to tell you about chapters 8 and beyond until sometime next year. B000005JA8|5.0|R.I.P. Don Glen Vliet aka Don Van Vliet aka CAPTAIN BEEFHEART!  He was humanly born of the WWII stoicism of 1941 (hey, Belushi made a movie by that title!) and grew on wings of music and giant legs of paint striding boldly into the 21st century (Schizoid Man!).  Then on like a madman he charged as a cave-painting of a bull through the evanescent worlds of art, music and pop culture making rock-n-roll records.  Thankfully for us, The Dear Captain crafted music and art and poetry that was blessedly less how THEY told you it was and more about the POSSIBILITIES OF IT ALL.  Beefheart's guitars & paintbrushes were singular, otherly-thinking messages, his Magic Bands and their aesthetically musical output, that ever-expanding magnum opus, TROUT MASK REPLICA: the masterpiece.  4 days ago, passed from us this lighter-than-air-but-heavier-than-thought shaman who swirled his deft Mojo Sticks in the Mojave Desert's rocket sauce and blithely re-painted a dusky musical landscape with Hobo Chang Ba, Hair Pie Bake I & II, and Neon Meate Dream of a Octafish...and that was just to start!  Captain Beefheart bent his unique sonic-vision of music upon the wills of utterly fantastic musicians who became in his sculptor's hands (perhaps unwillingly) scions of the rock-n-roll avant garde.  The dear Captain channeled from the depths of nature an impossibly ultra-human dialog and played it gloriously loud through dusty speakers to wincing listeners.  One can only imagine those live shows:  the Magic Band's twin guitars fought like light-sabres with wailing saxophones over oddly paced drums-n-bass then leavened and twisted by the grunted conjurings of a desert voodoo priest bent on painting the air with color-filled exhortations about fantastic, under-appreciated animals, quaint, ecological gardens and lot's of to-be-revered women.<br />  There is no sense in redescribing this music for you readers; others have done so admirably.  The best suggestion that can be made is that if you have hungry little earholes and are tired of what passes for new music these days:  dive right in.  It gets no more bizarre nor beautiful than Trout Mask Replica (ok, LICK MY DECALS OFF, BABY does so yet with a more rhythmic and lyrical finesse, and fewer caveman howls!).  If you can't give TMR three listens and crave three-hundred-and-thirty-three more, then MOVE ON, THERE IS NOTHING TO HEAR HERE.  But if you can patiently let this expression of pure id in, let it frighten/thrill/confuse you for all of it's 28 songs, you will have taken the sonic equivalent of a walk through a contemporary art museum, featuring a solo exhibition by the likes of Van Gogh, Picasso or Dali...AND SOMEHOW BE BETTER FOR IT.  But one needn't be an artist to appreciate this music (nor a musician, nor intellectual, nor misanthrope...tho, it may help).  Don't mistake the finely honed and muscular musicianship for addled cacophony:  wait for it.  TMR is not out of tune, merely out of time and place.  Captain Beefheart and his Magic Band have carved thoughtful, singing toy animals from joyful noise and earth just for you, little human.  So get your blues on, open up to the universe and be taken to that place of lore and artful chance that will be fondly recalled for the rest of time.ry little earholes and are tired of what passes for new music these days:  dive right in. 1400097029|4.0|Historian of art Max Morden's wife has just died of cancer. This novel explores his grief and his efforts to find himself again. What he does is to rent a room in The Cedars, a guest room -now in decadence- where Morden and his family used to pass their vacations and where, fifty years ago, Max fell in love one summer with Chloe, the daughter of the Graces, a wealthy family also stopping there. The novel is written in the first person, and it mixes three narrative threads: the first is Max's current situation, living alone in the decayed mansion, with the proprietor, the enigmatic Mis Vavasour, and the other guest, the Colonel, a manic old man. The second is his wife's Anna's last year, as well as the history of their mariage and their daughter, Claire. And the third is the story of that wonderful and tragic summer by the sea. The ending provides two big surprises.    It is easy to empathize with Max's loss even if the reader has not experienced a similar one (and most adults have lost, if not a love partner, some other kind of loved one). Banville is very good at transmiting the feeling of destitution, mixed with the ambivalent memories of married life, and the romantic evocations of long lost summers of wonder. Perhaps what makes it even more poignant is that Max is not a great character, an outstanding person of some kind, but a retired, rather common man just like everybody else. Definitely a melancholy novel, it is very beautiful, written by a great stylist, with very good character development, skilled at conveying the perplexity, the pain, and the traps set by memory.

In the age of texting and cell phones, the rumor travels at light speed and soon everyone believes Olive is the tramp of all tramps.<br /><br />Things get even more complicated after Olive goes off on Marianne's friend and ends up in detention. Fellow detentionee Brandon is constantly picked on because the school thinks he's gay, which he is. He just would rather not deal with the bullying. Olive lets Brandon know how the rumor started without any factual basis and suggest he do the same, telling people he scored with some female student. Of course he convinces the good hearted Olive into pretending she was his conquest. A fake liaison behind closed doors at a party and the word spreads once more.<br /><br />But this leads to more problems as Brandon tells various friends about the arrangement he had with Olive. Now more misfits come to her asking for the same favor paying with gift cards from various stores. The deeper Olive gets into the lie, the more Marianne seeks to remove her and her rumor inspired activities from the school. Not only that, Rhia turns her back on her friend when she takes center stage instead of her.<br /><br />All of this escalates to a point where the notoriety soon becomes more than Olive can handle. Toss in the lies affecting her favorite teacher and the dream of one day becoming involved with the boy she's had a crush on for years and you can see where this is going.<br /><br />The solution Olive comes up with is original and inspired (as I said) by the many movies John Hughes made involving teens in trouble like SIXTEEN CANDLES and PRETTY IN PINK with a nice play on the film SAY ANYTHING. These movies all presented us with a world where every teen character had a problem that was solved by the end of the film. The same happens here. It's the journey that makes it worth watching.<br /><br />The movie is funny, touching and a nice look at the world of teen rumors, gossiping and hurtful ways. With any luck, teens watching the film will come away thinking twice about the things they say and text.<br /><br />Emma Stone is marvelous here, offering that young girl quality so necessary to the character as well as the innocence of the things she sets in motion. Her smile lights up the screen when she has those moments where she gets to show the real character of Olive rather than the fake one she offers the students.<br /><br />The movie offers plenty of laughs and some tender moments as well while taking swipes at those who would consider themselves above the rest and morally superior to everyone around them. In truth, everyone has a side they'd rather not let the rest of the world see. In the character of Olive, we get a glimpse of what could happen if you created a side that didn't exist but others believed.<br /><br />For me the true test of a movie is whether or not I think I'd watch it again. This movie may not be Oscar material but it will remain on my shelf. It was fun. It made me smile. And you'll definitely have a good time watching it keeping in mind that it's not Shakespeare but just a movie and a good one at that.good time watching it keeping in mind that it's not Shakespeare but just a movie and a good one at that.

B0034JKZ86|5.0|THE PRINCESS AND THE FROG is a very loose adaptation of the classic fairy tale, THE FROG PRINCE.  Disney took the story of the prince who has been turned into a frog and can only be saved by a kiss, and launched a story all its own.  The story itself is pleasant enough, but frankly, it is nothing special.  What IS special about this film is Disney's return to a 2D, hand drawn animation style and the wonderful idea to set this film in bayou country in Louisiana.      First, the story.  In this film, a plucky but poor young lady named Tiana desires nothing more than to open a restaurant and prepare the food she and her dear departed father used to enjoy making for their friends and neighbors.  She has remained friends with spoiled but kind-hearted rich girl...and this puts her into contact with Prince Naveen, who is visiting New Orleans in search of a rich girl to marry (because his parents have cut him off from their money).  Unfortunately, by the time Tiana meets him, he's already been turned into a frog by the local voodoo specialist, Dr. Facilier.  He convinces Tiana to kiss him...but rather than break the spell, Tiana is ALSO turned into a frog.  This launches the two into an unlikely but fun adventure in the swamps, where they meet a friendly gator and a very amusing (and very Cajun) firefly.  Leave it to Disney to come up with great sidekicks.  And the idea of the fireflies is not only creative, but leads to some absolutely gorgeous scenes.    And those gorgeous scenes are rendered \\"old school\\".  I'm sure some of this was aided by computer...but the look is hand-drawn, just like the days of SNOW WHITE or SLEEPING BEAUTY.  While I heartily enjoy the Pixar films and many other 3D-esque animated features...PRINCESS AND THE FROG reminded me that these 2D images can feel like masterpieces of art come to life.  The lush and colorful backgrounds somehow feel more \\"real\\" than the sometimes photorealistic backgrounds of a film like TOY STORY.  I frequently found myselfsimply marveling at the gorgeous, watercolor backgrounds or the sumptuous, evocative color choices.  The whole film feels like a Mardi Gras.    The story certainly could have been told in other settings...but putting it in New Orleans at what feels like the time between the two World Wars was genius.  We see the stark contrasts between rich and poor.  Between black & white.  Yet New Orleans has a sparkle and energy that is unique...and that comes to vivid life too.  It's a melting pot like no other...and the film captures that mood and ambiance.  And this setting inspired Randy Newman to write some of his most un-Randy Newman-like music...and it works great.  I FREQUENTLY found myself tapping my toes to the beat.  The songs are simply terrific.  None really became breakout hits...but they all wonderfully accent the film.    The voice work is generally top notch.  A few famous folks drop by, such as Oprah Winfrey and the unmistakable John Goodman (both in small roles).  But led by relative unknown Anika Noni Rose as Tiana...we spend very little time \\"recognizing\\" famous voices, and most of the time appreciating the uniformly excellent acting and singing.      The film was a modest hit in theaters.  It just didn't generate huge excitement.  Heck, it took me over a year to see it.  But truly it is a throwback to Disney's glory days, and while it doesn't quite achieve the heights of PINOCCHIO or CINDERELLA...it is a very, very worthy entry into that pantheon.  While it's great for adults, please try to see it with a child.  They'll thank you.

There is probably an appropriate strip for about every other moment, and really, no other strip has come as close in terms of caliber of writing.  Watterson's stylish, visceral panels carried a lot of humor, a lot more feeling, and Hawkeye Pierce level of the sardonic.  Regardless of what I think of this strip, however, the books themselves are bound heavily and handsomely, and the 23 pound set is printed on heavy art paper; it is apparent that the same amount of love and care that went into the drawing and writing went into Watterson's considerations for putting together this set.  It is well-known that the only merchandise he has ever authorized has been the collections books, and so it is fitting that the entirety of the series should be conveyed in something with a little more physical weight than your average coffee table book.    Aside from the fact that this set is a book-end until itself (and there is a lot to be said for being able to put this on one end of a shelf and not have to worry about the other books falling over because what in the world but a second set of this type leaning could ever knock it over?), the warm ruddy brown exterior looks beautiful on a bookshelf and is eye-catching.    This is the kind of gift one gives a serious fan; everyone has met the kind of person who loves this comic strip, and it is we (those people) that appreciate this sort of thing best as we still have an appreciation for Calvin's world and Hobbes' one liners.  You certainly could spend the same money as this set costs on video games and the like, but I am quite certain that I have gotten more enjoyment out of these books than I ever would have out of some surely-frustrating game for a scant ten or 15 hours of gameplay.  In getting this set for younger readers, we might hope it imparts in them a desire for the lost activity of playing outside, which today seemsa wholly foreign concept; snowmen eating snowcones made from other snowmen do not build themselves.

006117758X|5.0|Ham on Rye is a book I've had kicking around the house for awhile but only got around to reading it this autumn. I thought--as an autobiography of Buk's early life--it would be dreary and depressing. I am pleased to report that I was totally wrong. Ham on Rye isn't disheartening but it is an incredibly poignant tale.<br /><br />Here Bukowski's writing is excellent; perhaps superior to anything else he penned at any other point in his career. His first memory was of being under a table...and we progress from there. I was reminded of a line from the musician Beck, \\"at a time of chimpanzees I was a monkey,\\" after reading that the author's dad bought him an Indian suit with accompanying bow and arrow...at a time when all the other kids wore cowboy outfits. It seems such an outcome was Buk's destiny. His father also discouraged him from reading books and ridiculed his early WWI based stories revolving around the figure of a neo-Red Baron.<br /><br />Buk grew up during the depression and his portrait of those days is something that most of us--born citizens within an America of bounty--will have difficulty imagining. People in those days had three mortgages, and instead of selling new shoes the department stores sold heels and glue in order to maintain the ones you already had. Each child possessed two pairs of pants: one for Sundays and the second for the other six days of the week. When you get to page 95 (chapter 22) that marks Buk's first illicit drink you long to hit the delete key in order to prevent the main character's descent into an addiction that largely poisoned his life. But, as you'd expect, the tale of Baldy and he drinking from barrels wine is fascinating...as are all the vignettes included in this underrated masterpiece. If you read only one book by Buk, this is the one I'd recommend.

However, I can't overcome some of the impossibilities such as crossing the Gobi desert for 16 days with little to no water and food, and crossing the Himalayas in their condition. A remarkable quote from this book is \\"when lost, freedom is difficult to regain.\\" That quote holds true for many men and women who have suffered through political oppression throughout the world.     Basically, The Long Walk is a nice piece of historical fiction.

B0039TD7RC|5.0|What a time in music. In 1971, two of the greatest rock and roll bands were working on masterpieces at the same time. Pete Townsend and The Who were working on what was then known as Lifehouse and The Rolling Stones were working on Exile On Main Street. Townsend was working on an incredibly ambitious concept album centering around the theme that, if there was an ultimate truth in the world, it would not be a verbal idea but a musical note. At the same time, The Stones, with business and tax problems leaving them broke and homeless at the height of their career, were working around no concept whatsoever, just going with the flow. And what were the results? Pete's grandiose concept was eventually abandoned, and what emerged out of the wreckage of trying to find life's answer was one of the greatest rock and roll albums of all time, Who's Next. And Exile On Main Street? It aimed for nothing, and perhaps ended up hitting upon maybe the truth Pete was looking for. If there is an ultimate truth in life, Exile on Main Street is as close as we'll ever come in Rock Music. I'm in my early 40s, and so I am too young for 60s style optimism, and like most of my generation, Sgt. Pepper is nothing to me but a pretty idea. That said, I am also technically too young for Exile On Main Street, as I never heard it until 1991. Yet it moves me like no album I have ever heard. And only now do I know why. As the country descends into an ever deepening economic and social malaise, and we are left looking back wondering \\"Was everything we believed wrong?\\" I've lost faith in most ideas, yet not in the message of \\"Exile\\" It takes a certain person to \\"Get it\\" just as it takes a certain kind of person to \\"Get\\" the Bible. For me, it's exactly what Rock and Roll is. I don't want to rank it, categorize it, or judge it. I just want to listen to it. It makes me want to sip whiskey (I don't like whiskey), smoke cigarettes (I don't smoke) and sing the incoherent lyrics. At many times in my life, it's the best answer there is. Try it. And if you don't \\"get it\\" the first time, try it again a few years later when things don't seem quite so clear anymore. It's dirty, messy, sad, and chaotic. And, at just the right times, incredibly beautiful.

1441866868|5.0|Paolo Bacigalupi's epic novel of future Thailand seems like the sort of thing that could be a description of how we live in the very near future.  Set some hundreds of years after the collapse of oil and the rising of the oceans, humanity has just begun to reestablish global trade using great sailing ships and dirigbles.  The most precious of all resources is the calorie, as living beings powered by food & water are the most portable energy source available in a world without carbon fuel.<br /><br />The story throws us straight into the life of a calorie-man, the employee of a North American super-corporation who is secretly spying upon the homegrown food of Thailand.  You see, most natural foods have been wiped out, either by accidental plagues, or the occasional purpouseful scourge desgined to increase demand for genetically modified foods.  In this environemnt a seed bank of uncontaminated crops is more valuable, and more powerful, than nuclear weapons.<br /><br />Calorie-man Anderson's story of industrial espionage and hard-scrabble surivival on the mean streets of Bangkok is contrasted with the stories of a idealistic government enviornmental enforcer, Jaidee.  Jaidee is famous for defying the corporations and his own government's trade ministry in his relentless dedication to environmental protections.  Imagine if Dirty Harry worked for the EPA, and you have something close.  He fearlessly burns illegal food imports, and his squads beat violators without pity.  He has many enemies, foreign corporations and power hungry factions of the Thai government, and his loyal number 2 Kanya may not be what she seems.<br /><br />Weaving throughout these stories are other characters, including the eponymous Windup Girl, a Japanese \\"New Person,\\" genetically engineered to be a better version of humanity, but crippled by genetic and indoctrinated obedience and submission.  Emiko, the Windup Girl, is conditioned to be the perfect servent, but even genetic conditioning can be weakened by a lifetime of abuse and humiliation.  She dreams of escaping Bangkok to find a rumored settlement of free New People, deep in the plague-ravanged jungles.<br /><br />Bacigalupi extrapolates the current tensions between the have & have-not nations, the environmental fears, and the unsolved energy problems to create a world that seems both alien and familiar.  He doesn't invent some magical new technology to save the planet.  Instead, human ingenuity and perserverence are the only things that keep people alive in the face of an increasingly hostile environment.  Everything is harder that it used to be, and the people all know it.  There are dreams of a return to the old ways before the collapse of global energy, but most of the time people are content to simply get by without being killed by a plague, or in the crossfire of government and corporations.  Bacigalupi's prose is simple and evocative, making you feel the contrast between the relentless humidity and filth of the Bangkok slums and the cool, clean technology that still exists deep within corporate offices.<br /><br />Jonathan Davis reads the story with passion and eloquence, keeping the hard to pronounce Thai names clear and easy to follow for my American ears.  He gives each character a distinctive voice, and it is never hard to follow the reading.  The audio edition also includes an introduction by Bacigalupi, who describes the genesis of the novel from a fever-dream experience in a South Asian hotel room.e of abuse and humiliation. B0036EBARO|1.0|There's a reason this is the lowest rated album by Goldfrapp. Whether you like this album or not will be dictated by how you feel about 80s synth-pop. And not the edgy, atmospheric 80s sound that has influenced some of their previous music -- think mindless, peppy, Top 40 bubblegum pop you might hear at a cheerleader rally. Consistent through Goldfrapp's previous four albums has been their ability to create infectious and at times beautiful tunes, and you can even hear some of that talent in these songs. Unfortunately, it's all buried under a thick layer of retro, saccharine production, and for the most part the lyrics are what you might expect a 13 year old to scribble on her lunch napkin. It's hard to know if this album represents an ironic pose or a calculated attempt at selling units, but whatever the intent, listening to it is like eating a plateful of Twinkies -- it's good for the first minute, then leaves you feeling queasy, unfulfilled, and sorry you ever had the idea in the first place. One good song somehow was accidentally included (Hunt), but if you're not familiar with this group, please try any of their other albums before listening to this. Here's hoping they execute yet another stylistic about-face on their next outing; just about anything would be better than this.

B000002L9J|5.0|The Queen Is Dead was the fourth album released by Manchester band, The Smiths, and is believed by many to be the best thing that this incredibly influential band ever recorded. The Smiths have a trademark sound of Jonny Marr's ability to write wonderfully catchy pop songs, while Morrissey sings his typically cynically mournful, but yet humourous lyrics, in a manner that showcases his wit and intelligence. The Smiths remain one of the largest inspirations for many indie bands today.<br /><br />If you are at all familiar with the music of The Smiths, you will know that Morrissey's lyrics are part of the same group as those of the likes of Thom Yorke and Roger Waters; looking at everyday life with often cynical humor, creating music that many people consider depressing. Often underrated is the rhythm section of the band, which provides a very good base for all the songs. Overall, I consider this album to be a classic of the genre, and one that, even if you have never heard The Smiths, but like Indie music, is an absolute must to check out.

0684833395|3.0|I don't presume to add much to the varied and detailed reviews of the book, except perhaps for an observation on how a popular work esteemed by one generation simply may not transcend its own time.<br />Like many, I first read Catch-22 many years ago in high school. I don't believe it was assigned; rather, it was one of those revolutionary, counter-cultural books that you just HAD to read (Like One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest). At the time, the Vietnam War was winding down, the \\"counter-culture\\" had come and was rapidly going, and unbounded faith in the wisdom and integrity of authority (especially governmental authority) seemed dubious, at best. Although it was published a number of years prior to that, Catch-22 seemed a brilliant evocation of everything we KNEW was wrong with authority, the govenment, war (read, Vietnam) and capitalism.  War was wasteful, individuals were largely pawns in a much bigger game, rapacious capitalists would deal with either or both sides to make a buck, and the underlying logic of the entire equation could only be described as \\"absurd,\\" or, perhaps, a rational man could only grasp the world, and continue to function with his reason intact, if he came to appreciate absurdity.<br />I still believe Catch-22 brilliantly makes all of these points. I will never forget, for example, Milo Minderbinder's seemely bizarre schemes to make money for the \\"syndicate\\" by cornering the market in cotton and trying to produce from it something edible, or contracting with the other side to bomb his own army. Similarly, the concept of Catch-22 (only a crazy man would want to fly more missions, and if you are crazy, we can't let you fly more missions; but you don't want to fly more missions, so you're not crazy, so you have to fly) has become a part of the language, though I'm not sure everyone who uses the term these days (fewer do) could explain its meaning or origin.  Finally, I love the idea that, because time seems to pass more quickly when you are bored, the best way to extend your life is to bore yourself unmercifully.<br />All of that being said, I do believe that many of the more negative reviews of the book result from our familiarity with so much of the political landscape of the book that, 40 years ago or more, seemed positively shocking, if not revolutionary.  Ours is not an especially revolutionary time, so Heller's absurdity, on the one hand, seems a bit out of step.  More than that, it is no longer new; we've heard it all before.  Reading Catch-22 now seeems a bit like listening to an overly long joke that we've heard too many times. Finally, it is hard for us to appreciate how unique the book seemed in the '60s and  70's as a critique of war, especially a critique of World War II--the Last Good War of the Great Generation.  Before Heller, the literature coming out of WWII, despite depictions of avarice or cruelty or waste, had none the less esteemed that war as a great and good thing that, in pertinant part, overthrew an evil dictator and freed (part of) Europe, etc.  It was shocking to see THAT war depicted in THIS way.<br />I only came to indulge in this review because, recently, I thought I would re-read the book. I was hoping I would find it fresh and funny and insightful (as I remembered it).  I was disappointed. It was still occasionally funny and made many of the points I remembered. But the disjointed nature of the narrative seemed confusing and indulgent, whereas I remembered it as groundbreaking.  And much of the book left me rather cold. It seemed long-winded, and repetitive (because we really did get the point), and many of the characters seem as if they are there only to set up a joke or make a point and they are not especially real or multidimensional.<br />In any case, I would still recommend reading the book. Like many \\"popular\\" or \\"political\\" books, however, to really appreciate it requires casting your mind back to a different time and looking at it from that perspective. Without doing so, it is hard to realize how unique and deservedly influential the book was.  On the other hand, if you cannot or will not approach the book from that perspective (and it is certainly no sin if you cannot), I'm afraid a new reader will find himself agreeing with the more negative reviews on this site.  That seems a shame, even though I can hardly imagine Heller or any of his earliest readers contemplating that the book would ever be a historical curiosity or an expression of the mind-set of its day rather than a penetrating satire.realize how unique and deservedly influential the book was.  On the other hand, if you cannot or will not approach the book from that perspective (and it is certainly no sin if you cannot), I'm afraid a new reader will find himself agreeing with the more negative reviews on this site.  That seems a shame, even though I can hardly imagine Heller or any of his earliest readers contemplating that the book would ever be a historical curiosity or an expression of the mind-set of its day rather than a penetrating satire.

B004132I4S|5.0|It's hard to write a decent review for one of the most shining, innovative albums of the year. That being said, I've been tentatively been putting this review on the back burner. Before I begin, I have to tell you I wasn't much of a Sufjan fan before. His voice was soft spoken and songs felt a little too childish and unrelatable for my alternative rock tastes. But Sufjan's new album, entitled \\"Age of Adz,\\" blew my preconceived notions out of the water and left me gasping on the beach. Not only has Sufjan reinvented his song writing, he has managed to do so with utter  ingenuity  and brilliance. I've heard comparisons to Radiohead's \\"Kid A,\\" but it's more of a step-brother to \\"Hail to the Thief,\\" albeit better. Many of Age of Adz mirrors paintings of a schizophrenic artist and his own battle with a immune disease.     The intro Futile Devices is more of a farewell to elegance of the past. \\"Too Much\\" sets the mood right, to the seemingly eclectic and bizarreness of the album. Age of Adz seems like a brilliant summary from the album as a whole,  beginning like a monstrous nightmare at a circus, to beautiful guitar strings at the end. \\"I Walked\\" is probably the black sheep of all the songs, repetitive and mundane.  What is most notable about Sufjan's singing and his lyrics is that they are the most poignantly honest and emotional in his career. Songs like \\"Now That I'm Older,\\" laments past mistakes and \\"All for Myself\\" is starkly introspective. \\"Vesuvius\\" explores the darkest crevices of human nature and Sufjan's increasing desperation. And my favorite song, \\"I Want To Be Well\\" pushes the listener to borderline insanity-- sweet, insane, pandemonium of a mind that isn't \\"f-ing around.\\" And who can forget the 25 minute song that is a bit self-indulging, but worth an admirable ending.      Somehow Sufjan has able to craft an ingeniously dark, hopeful, deep, and intimately album that demands careful and repetitive listening. The album sure isn't easy listening, nor \\"radio friendly,\\" but like a fine wine it'll require aging. You'll be hooked.

This is an achievement few other programming books can claim.  While meta-programming in ruby is certainly a pleasure when compared to other oo languages, it is still a rather arcane subject, and for this author to be able to explain it on a deep level to beginners is simply awe inspiring!  There is also plenty of material for the advanced rubyist including a chapter devoted to what I like to call \\"dynamic adaptive programming\\" but will be known more familiarly as \\"code that writes code\\".  But even the expert will want to read the whole text for its clear, almost zen-like treatment of meta-programming. 0375701427|4.0|Philip Roth's Pulitzer Prize Winning novel uniquely chronicles the downfall of the great American family of the 50's and the cataclysmic cultural transformation of the 60's generation as they forcefully broke away from their parents' values.      The novel tells the story of \\"Swede\\" Levov through the eyes of Nathan Zuckerman, Roth's frequent narrator and someone who worshipped Swede as a child.  Swede has it all on the outside.  He grew up a high school legend in Newark, an athlete who excelled at everything he did, a tall attractive blond who stuck out in the Jewish neighborhood he grew up in.        After graduation, Swede joins the Marines, hopeful to get in on the action in WWII, but enlists just as the war ends.  He then goes to a small NJ college to play baseball, and is good enough to play in the Major Leagues.  However, he sacrifices that career to work for his father, who owns a glove-making business.  (A metaphor of the cultural changes to come as gloves transform from a daily garment to a niche product.)  As a consolation prize however, he ends up with a beautiful wife--Miss New Jersey 1949, in fact--and a wonderful young daughter named Merry, whose only flaw seems to be a stutter.       The fabric begins to unravel however when Merry begins to have anger issues.  She lashes out at her parents about Vietnam and the comfortable lives they lead.  Eventually she becomes uncontrollable, spending weekends in NYC despite her father's weak protests.  Finally a bomb is set off in their small-town post office, killing one person.  Merry disappears, so is immediately implicated.  From there we follow Swede over the next five years as his family and the idyllic life he had always dreamed of falls apart.        What struck me about reading this novel was that I felt I had no idea what would happen next, not just from a perspective of plot, but also form.  There's a feeling of suspense even though there aren't many true scenes--much time is spent in Swede's turbulentmind.  But from Swede's disturbing meeting with Rita Cohen to the final dinner scene, I was amazed at where Roth took me; he was masterful in his scene selection.      I agree with other reviews that have complained about the book dragging in parts.  This happens mostly when we're inside Swede's head, which becomes more bogged down with thoughts as the story progresses, and at the glove factory, where the manufacture of the gloves and the business is lovingly reflected in all its fine detail.  Yes, there were times I just wanted Roth to move on.      Still, I think most thoughtful readers will see what he was trying to do, and that is to write a deep and intelligent chronicle of one of the most crushing cultural disturbances in the last century through the eyes of a man who had the most to lose from it.  From the use of Zuckerman to tell of his boyhood idol's downfall, to the climactic dinner party, where Watergate, Deep Throat, infidelity, alcoholism, and religion all make appearances, Roth approaches the subject in a unique and gifted way.  This is book is definitely worth the acclaim.

B0040QYROK|5.0|To those individuals who are concerned about this latest (complete) version of \\"Metropolis,\\" you may go to the Kino.com website where they describe the most recent restoration of the film, that includes the additional 25 minutes of material found in the Argentinean film archives.  As for New Zealand material, I was able to find the following information on Wikipedia.com:    \\"In 2005, Wollongong-based historian and politician Michael Organ examined a print of the film in the National Film Archive of New Zealand. It had been thought that it was the same cut as the Australian version, but Organ discovered that it contained missing scenes not seen in the cut versions of the film. After hearing of the discovery of the Argentine print of the film and the restoration project currently under way, Organ contacted the German restorers about his find. The New Zealand print was found to contain 11 missing scenes and included seconds of footage which were missing from the Argentine print and also footage which could be used to restore damaged sections of the Argentine print. It is believed that the editor in charge of editing the New Zealand print for some unknown reason excised different scenes than that of the Australian print keeping scenes missing from other versions intact. It is believed that the Australian, New Zealand and Argentine prints were all scored from the same master. The newly discovered footage was used in the restoration project.\\"    So, unless another lost version emerges this latest incarnation would appear to be as good as it is going to get.

0143113100|5.0|I had the great fortune of finding this book at a thrift store, mixed in with a bunch of worthless old novels. It was one dollar, so I thought \\"what the heck,\\" and picked it up. As I read through it I became increasingly amazed. The scientific content, writing style, and captivating stories combine to make this a top-tier popular science book. It is a great mixture of 1) raw facts about how brain plasticity works and how it is changing medicine, 2) fascinating stories of people overcoming serious neural problems by harnessing the power of neuroplasticity, and 3) inspiration for people who want to make changes in their life--for it clearly illustrates that the brain is capable of radical transformations.<br /><br />One of the wildest neuroplasticity stories is about a woman who completely lost her vestibular system (structures in the ears that allow your body to balance). This woman then regained her balance through the use of a new invention: a strip that sat on her tongue and informed her brain--through sensory nerves in the tongue--which direction she was leaning. The strip buzzes on the part of the tongue that corresponds with the direction the body is leaning, and somehow the brain is capable of using this information to create a new balance system! Another story, which seems to straddle that line between science and science fiction, is about a device that allows a blind person to see by buzzing on their back. A camera transmutes sensory information onto a grid of nodes that buzz in particular ways that correspond to the various properties of the visual field. The brain recieves this buzzing information through the sensory nerves of the back. Over time, this gives blind people (who were blind at birth) a primitive visual ability. How on earth does this work? As the supergenius who came up with the device says, \\"The eyes don't see. It is the brain that sees.\\" Wow!<br /><br />Other books that I have read about the brain (specifically Religion Explained by Pascal Boyer) stress the fact that the brain is composed of distinct modules that perform specific tasks. Although this is true, arguments that emphasize modularity can mislead readers into thinking that brain functions are completely fixed in specific locations. The reality is that the brain is modular, but also malleable. Brain functions can change locations in the brain, can take up more or less room in the brain, can be strengthened and revitalized after being severely damaged, and so on. This book gives many fascinating examples of such malleability.<br /><br />Yet in addition to offering intellectual stimulation, these facts also provide hope to those who struggle. I have personally struggled for a long time with a variety of psychological problems, namely depression, and this book has given me more hope than any other. I started meditating after reading this book because it convinced me that I have the power to actually physically change my brain--or, rather, that my brain has the power to change itself.<br /><br />What really makes this book a pleasure to read is that it is comprised of highly interesting stories. Each story pertains to neuroplasticity (brain malleability), but each is also interesting in its own right. Doidge, a practicing psychiatrist, gives an incredible personal account of treating one of his own patients who suffered from a disorder based on his mother's early death. He also tells stories about top brain scientists, describing their discoveries and their lives with the tact of a master journalist. It's fun to read, and highly educational.fact that the brain is composed of distinct modules that perform specific tasks. Although this is true, arguments that emphasize modularity can mislead readers into thinking that brain functions are completely fixed in specific locations. The reality is that the brain is modular, but also malleable. 0439023483|5.0|So after months of seeing nothing but blog post after blog post for the release of Mockingjay, the third book in The Hunger Games Trilogy, after hearing a very close friend rave about the series, I caved. I went out and bought the first book, The Hunger Games by Suzanne Collins.<br /><br />In the post-apocalyptic world, where North America once lay, is the country of Panem. With it's shining Capitol at the center of twelve districts. The Capitol rules all. The very lives of the people are not even under their own control, they live and die by the Capitol's generosity and greed.<br /><br />Seventy-five years ago there were thirteen districts, and there was a rebellion. The districts rose up against the rule of the Capitol. And were squashed. District 13 was destroyed utterly and the other twelve live an even more bleak existence. Since then, the Capitol, to remind the people of their absolute power and the futility of dissent, has required that each district provide two tributes. One girl and one boy between the ages of twelve and eighteen are taken to compete in the Hunger Games. A nationally televised event that pits these children against each other, and only one can come out alive.<br /><br />When sixteen-year-old Katniss Everdeen steps forward to take her place in the Seventy-Fourth Hunger Games she regards it as a death sentence. Having to feed and provide for her mother and younger sister for the last several years has made Katniss persevere. She already knows how to fight to live, how to endure difficulties, and it will take every inch of her abilities in order to gain success. But if she's going to win, Katniss is going to face hard decisions. Life against love. Survival against humanity. And at the end of it all, if she actually manages to succeed, will she be someone she can live with?<br /><br />Because I know there are people out there that haven't read these yet, I'm going to try to remain spoiler free. I really believe that this series is one that's best enjoyed as it unfolds. Suzanne Collins did a wonderful job of pulling me along and wringing emotions out of me, from smiles, to tears, to outright shock.<br /><br />Katniss has had a hard life. She lost her father quite a while before the story begins and because her mother was so stricken with grief, it fell to Katniss to make sure that her family lived. She became the main provider, going outside the electrified fences that surround the district in order to hunt. Learning to trade and bargain. Selling and buying in order to keep her sister from ever knowing the depth of despair she had. She's a strong heroine, but she's not perfect.<br /><br />She makes mistakes, misreads people and their objectives - even when it seems so clear to everyone else - and she can be exceptionally hard-headed. I admit that there were times when I wanted to shake her to make her see the truth of something, but in the end all it did was endear her more to me. Katniss is a heroine you can root for. Flaws and all.<br /><br />The relationships between Katniss and everyone around her was something I truly enjoyed. For all her rough edges, Katniss has a very big, soft heart and you could easily see that in her thoughts and conversations. From her family, to her mentor for the Games, to her fellow tribute, even other competitors in the arena, each interaction brought more...just more of everything. There were several times I was moved to tears and sometimes I was smiling through them.<br /><br />Beyond the characters, which I absolutely fell in love with, the world surrounded me in its embrace, holding me tight and showing me everything. I felt involved there, connected to the people and the place. More than anything I wanted to fight alongside them, with them, for them. Ms. Collins paints a vivid picture of a dark and gritty world, brushed with realism that, quite honestly, hit me like a punch to the gut.<br /><br />With my emotions, frequently, running high I raced through The Hunger Games anxious to see what would happen next. It kept me on the edge of my seat, staying up late into the night. In fact, I finished it around midnight on a Friday, fell asleep and woke up to immediately go to the store to buy the next two. Which I promptly read.<br /><br />The Hunger Games is a dark, gritty, emotional story filled with pain, joy, loss and love. I definitely recommend it to anyone looking to get lost in a vivid world filled with characters that make you feel and a plot that doesn't let you go.y seat, staying up late into the night.
